Design of an interface for remote monitoring of critical deviations in 
the daily activities of a frail elderly home-dwelling family member
This thesis is the final project of the MSc programme in Design & Innovation. The thesis is 
conducted with DTU management at the Technical University of Denmark, in fulfilment of the 
requirements to obtain a M.Sc.Eng. The project was carried out in the 5 months’ period from 
August 26, 2019 to January 26 2020.
Students:
Andreas Villadsen
Ditte Jørgensen 
Supervisor: 
Henning Boje Andersen
==PAGE:4==
 
==PAGE:5==
Abstract 
Lack of oversight of a frail home-dwelling elderly can in some
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
cases lead to critical events going unnoticed. Consequences of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which are often costly, irreversible, or even fatal. Thus, many
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives of frail elderly persons living alone, often experience
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
feelings of worry and are burdened by concerns related to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
well-being of the elderly. ​Existing research proposes to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alleviate these problems by use of in home monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technologies. However, most of the proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
systems target healthcare professionals and not the relatives
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as main users. Therefore, this thesis strives to explore the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design of a user interface, of an in home monitoring system,
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
targeting relatives as the main users.  
 
Particularly, the thesis adopts a user centric design thinking
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
approach,
to
investigate
how
a UI and its underlying
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor-system should be designed, in order to alleviate the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
worries and concerns experienced by the relatives and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly. Through semi-structured interviews with 7 relatives
 
 
 
 
  
 
and
14
elderlies,
user
needs
and
requirements
were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigated. The focus being to empathize with users through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their stories, worries and perceptions of the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring
system.
Additionally,
three
loops
of
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototype-facilitated user tests were conducted to uncover UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
specific user needs and requirements. 
 
 
 
The resulting UI design proposal, is a minimalistic teal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
coloured user interface for smartphones, named SenseFriend.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The UI revolves around a dashboard with a status indicator,
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
communicating the level of deviation in behavioural trends.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Furthermore, the UI contains a log of the latest activities and
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
an overview of the weekly and monthly trends in specific
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activities. ​Based on 3 rounds of evaluation and testing with
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
users, it is safe to assume that the proposed UI design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
represents
the
test
participants’
essential
needs
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirements. However, whether or not, the proposed UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design
encapsulates
the
average
relatives’
needs
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirements, cannot be concluded from this project.

Reading guide 
The thesis consists of 7 chapters in total. In the first chapter,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intro​, the thesis project is introduced and outlined and in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
last chapter, ​outro​, the thesis project is concluded and reflected
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
upon. The 5 chapters in between are structured according to
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the process of the project, where each chapter represent a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
specific phase. Each chapter has its own specific colour, and in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the beginning of each chapter a model will indicate the phase
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
of the process. In the end of each chapter, the particular phase
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are
wrapped
up
with
a
discussion,
reflection
and
a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sub-conclusion.  
  
Throughout the thesis, appendices are referenced with a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
simple code in square brackets, indicating the number of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
chapter and sequence. Eg. [A 3.2] will reference the second
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
appendix in the third chapter. The appendices are compiled in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a separate document, enabling the reader to investigate the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
appendices alongside the reading. We will encourage the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reader to do so, as it can enhance the understanding of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
process. Though, it is still possible to read and understand the
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thesis without the appendices.  
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
External literature is referenced using the citation style
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
American Psychological Association 6th edition (APA). The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
author and year are referenced in brackets directly in the text,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
like: (Norman, 2013).  
 
In
the
following,
a
glossary
of
the
used
terms
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
abbreviations are presented, and can be used throughout the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reading to support the reader’s comprehension.  
 
Regarding figures and tables, if they do not contain a source
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
reference in the figure/table text, the content is original work
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
from the thesis team.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:8==
 
Terms 
 
Relative 
The relative to an elderly family member or a friend that is 
providing care to the elderly. (caregiver, next of kin)  
 
Elderly family member  
The elderly person, whom the relative is worried about. It 
could also be a friend.  (care receiver) 
 
Fall alarm  
A device that enables the elderly to notify emergency services 
with the click of a button.  (Fall pendant, faldalarm, nødkald) 
 
Safety visit/call 
A home care service offered to check up on elderly people 
worried about falling or other critical events. It can both be in 
the form of a visit or a phone call. 
(tryghedsbesøg/tryghedsopkald) 
 
Multimodality 
Refers to the use of more than one mode. In the context used in 
this thesis, it refers to the use of more than one sensor/sensor 
type collaborating for the same specific purpose.

1. INTRODUCTION 
In this chapter the thesis project will be introduced and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
clarified. In the following, the project origin, scope and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
research questions will be presented. Furthermore, the thesis
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
process and plan will be presented. 
1.1. Project origin  
The project idea originates from a thesis proposal put forward
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
by the thesis supervisor, Henning Boje Andersen. The proposal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
arose based on his work carried out in the EU funded project,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Responsive Engagement of elderly promoting Activity and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Customized Healthcare (REACH). The REACH project revolves
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
around the idea of a remote monitoring system developed to
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
be used by professional caregivers (REACH 2020, n.d.).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, while working on the REACH project, it became clear
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
to him that there might also be a user need and a market
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
potential for a system targeted the relatives of frail elderly
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
people instead of healthcare professionals. This resulted in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
following thesis proposal:  
Design of a dashboard to monitor remotely daily activities of a
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
frail elderly home-dwelling family member. 
For us as students, the thesis proposal was especially
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interesting, as one of the main goals for our thesis project was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that it should be people centred. Furthermore, we found the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposal relatable as lack of oversight of an elderly family
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
member has caused problems in our own families. Therefore,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
we found it obvious to seize the opportunity and adopt the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thesis proposal. Additionally, ​most of the research focused on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring
systems
for
elderly
is
targeted
healthcare
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
professionals or home care. This makes monitoring systems
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
targeted
the
relatives
a
relatively
under-explored,
but
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dynamic, and promising area. Therefore, going into the project
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
we were motivated by both academic curiosity, and strong
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
empathy towards the context of the problem. Ultimately, we
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
also saw the project as a unique opportunity to investigate and
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
potentially add to this relatively novel area. 
1.2. Project scope 
The main purpose of the project is to explore the design of a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
user interface (UI), exploiting ambient in-home sensors,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aiming to provide peace of mind for relatives worrying about a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
frail elderly family member living alone. In other words, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
purpose is to explore the design of a UI and the underlying
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
sensor system, of an ambient assisted living system (AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system) able to monitor and detect critical events and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviations in behavioural trends of a frail elderly person living
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
alone. In this regard, the main focus of the project is on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
user
needs, requirements, and user experience of said
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
4 
==PAGE:13==
 
monitoring system. The project will not only be centered
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
around the visual presentation of data but also the types of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
data that users wish to have access to, possibilities of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
configuration, as well as the usability of the UI and the sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system. 
 
The ​primary user of the proposed monitoring system is relative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
concerned with the well-being of a frail elderly person living
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
alone. However, a sensor system will be installed in the home
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the frail elderly person, thus making them a ​secondary user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
of the proposed monitoring system. The project will therefore
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
also focus on including the needs and requirements of the frail
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly person in the context of the design. 
 
Besides affecting the primary and secondary users, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed monitoring system will potentially impact different
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
external stakeholders. Therefore, the project also aims to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discuss the likely impacts of the proposed monitoring system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on both an individual, organisational and a societal level. 
1.3. Research questions 
The research focus of the thesis project is expressed through a
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
main problem statement and 4 specific research questions. 
 
 
Problem statement 
What are the key features of a monitoring system that will
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
enable relatives, of a frail elderly family member living alone, to
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
detect critical events and deviations in behavioral trends and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thereby relieve worries by both the relatives and the elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
family member? 
 
Research questions 
●
What is the current status and history of ambient assisted
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
living (AAL) systems that target the detection of critical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
events and deviations in activities of frail elderly citizens? 
 
●
How would a pilot study of a proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system, utilising interviews and prototype-facilitated user
 
 
 
 
 
 
tests, uncover user needs and requirements? 
 
●
Which user interface design would fulfill the essential
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
user needs and requirements for the proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system? 
 
●
What are the likely societal impacts on a micro, meso and
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
macro level of monitoring systems of the proposed type? 
 
 
5 
==PAGE:14==
 
1.4. Thesis Process  
The thesis project has been divided into 5 phases; ​background
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
investigation, user research, definition of the sensor-system, UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design​ and ​discussion, ​see figure 1. 
 
 
The aim of the ​background investigation phase was to gain
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
knowledge about the problem context and the technological
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
domain surrounding the thesis. This phased helped creating a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
knowledge baseline for the subsequent phases of the thesis. In
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
this report, the background investigation is represented in the
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
form of a descriptive background chapter introducing the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reader to the context of the thesis project.  
  
The aim of the ​user research ​phase was to investigate both
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the relatives’ and the elderlies’ user needs and requirements
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for the proposed monitoring system. The user research was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
carried out through semi-structured interviews aimed at
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understanding their stories, worries and perceptions of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed system.  
  
Learnings
from the user research and the background
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigation was used in the ​definition of the sensor-system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phase. In this phase, the underlying sensor-system of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed monitoring system was defined in order to have a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
specific baseline to develop the UI design from.  
  
The aim of the ​UI design phase was to develop a proposal for a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
UI design for the proposed monitoring system, fulfilling the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
essential user needs and requirements. The phase was carried
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
out with an iterative approach where the UI was developed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and refined through 3 loops of prototype facilitated user tests.
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
Besides developing a specific UI design, this phase also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
provided a deeper understanding of the user needs and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirements of the proposed monitoring system.  
  
Lastly, the aim of the ​discussion phase was to discuss likely
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
benefits and challenges related to the proposed system on both
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a micro, meso and macro level, with the aim of understanding
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
what impacts the proposed system could have on the society. 
  
To plan and structure the thesis throughout the project, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
Gantt chart has been used. The Gantt chart has been revised
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concurrently with the development of the process plan. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
first edition of the Gantt chart can be seen in [A 1.1], while the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
final edition can be seen in [A 1.2].  
  
The report is structured according to the phases of the process
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and besides an intro and outro chapter, each chapter will
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
present a specific phase of the process. 
 
 
 
6 
==PAGE:15==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 1 - The 5 main phases of the thesis project

1.5. Theoretical framework  
In this section, the theoretical framework behind the thesis is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
described. For better readability, the specific theories and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
methods used throughout the thesis will be introduced in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
corresponding chapters.  
1.5.1. Design thinking 
The design process and problem solving approach of the thesis
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
has mainly been driven by the Design Thinking framework.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Design Thinking can in its essence be described as a solution
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
development framework with a human/user-centric focus
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Plattner et al., 2010). From the approach presented in Brown
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
& Wyatt (2010), 5 stages for working with the Design Thinking
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
framework can be derived: 
 
1. Empathize ​- Seeking to understand the users needs,
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
pains and feelings. 
2. Define - Putting the user needs in relation to a problem
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
that can be solved. 
3. Ideate ​- Exploring the solution space and proposing a
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
solution. 
4. Prototype ​- Building a solution that meets parts of the
  
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
user needs. 
5. Test - Testing the solution with users in order to gather
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
valuable feedback. 
 
Even though the framework is described in stages, Design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Thinking does ​not ​require the designers to follow a linear,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
stage-gate process. Instead, Design Thinking enables projects
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to loop back through the stages as the project evolves if so
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
desired (Brown & Wyatt, 2010). This is apparent in the thesis
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
project, as the last 3 stages of the design thinking framework
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
has been worked through in iterations, see figure 2. 
 
 
 
Fig. 2 - How an iterative UX design process can be adopted to the  5 
stages of the design thinking framework. 
 
The thesis project has developed through the 5 stages of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design thinking in the following ways; Initially it was sought to
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
Empathize with potential users by conducting semi structured
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interviews with both relatives and elderly and by investigating
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the problem context. The identified user needs and pains were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
8 
==PAGE:17==
 
then analysed and used to create themes and user personas,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which were used to ​Define the problem and solution space.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Thereby giving the project a direction and focus to ​Ideate,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
Prototype ​and ​Test solutions to the right problems. By testing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
UI prototypes new user insights were gained and assumptions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
eliminated. With new knowledge, a new loop of ​Ideation,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Prototyping and ​Testing were conducted. This process was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
completed 3 times in total before developing a final UI design
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
proposal. 
1.5.2. Lean UX design 
Besides the design thinking approach, the approach for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
developing the UI design has been heavily inspired by the lean
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
UX design approach presented by Klein (2013). The approach
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
revolves around testing ideas and hypotheses as quickly and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
often as possible. ​The lean loop is presented as a framework to
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
do so, see figure 3. ​The lean loop ​revolves around 3 steps,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Building, Measuring ​and ​Learning, that should be iterated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
throughout a design process in order to develop the ​product,
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
gather ​data ​and gain ​Ideas​. The steps making up the lean loop
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
can be translated directly into the 3 stages; ideate, prototype
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
and test, of the Design Thinking framework. Hence using ​the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
lean loop ​as a foundation for the UI design phase translated
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
well into the Design thinking framework used in the thesis
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project. 
 
Fig. 3 - “The Lean loop” graphic presented in the book “UX for lean 
startups” (Klein, 2013) 
 
Additionally, the ​lean UX ​approach to the UI design phase fit
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the relatively short time-frame of the thesis project, as it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enabled us to develop a user-driven and user-validated UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design in a short time frame.  
 
 
 
9 
==PAGE:18==
 
2. BACKGROUND 
INVESTIGATION 
In this chapter, key background information relevant for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
problem context and technological domain of the thesis is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented. The aim of this phase was to establish a knowledge
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
baseline to support the subsequent phases of the thesis. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
10 
==PAGE:19==
 
2.1. Methodology 
In this section, the specific methods, tools, and approaches
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applied in the background investigation phase are described. 
2.1.1. Literature search  
To find relevant literature a number of different search
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
methods were applied. First a block search method was used,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
where different search blocks were generated from the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
following stated search questions: 
 
●
How does the demographic development in Europe
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
impact the healthcare system and the support of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly generations? 
 
●
How does hospitalization or long-term illness affect the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
wellbeing of an elderly? 
 
●
How does relatives obligation to help an elderly family
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
member impact their quality of life? 
 
●
What is the current outlook on telemedicine and welfare
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technologies for elder care? 
 
●
What state of the art research has been done on remote
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activity monitoring of frail elderly people? 
●
What technological solutions are applicable in remote
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activity monitoring of elderly people? 
 
For each question, a work document was made, where
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
important search facets and search terms were listed. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
facets were then divided into blocks, containing all the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different terms. The work documents for each question,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
including the specific search terms can be seen in [A 2.1]. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
block search strategy did however not prove to be successful,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as it was unable to produce search results of relevant articles.
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
When changing the search terms and either adding or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
removing blocks, we found either too few articles or too many
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
irrelevant articles. However, the method worked great as a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
brainstorm method for search terms. The identified search
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
terms were utilised in a random search strategy, which gave
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
more relevant articles.  
  
The best strategy was found to be the citation pearl growing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
approach, where citations of fundamental and relevant articles
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were used to find more relevant articles on the desired topic.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
This approach was then applied every time a new relevant
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
article was found. The databases used for the literature search
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
has mainly been DTU find it, Web of Science and PubMed. In
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
total, the literature search resulted in 80 relevant articles,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which are all included in the background investigation. 
 
11 
==PAGE:20==
 
Additionally, to investigate the market, searches on both
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
google and YouTube was made with captions like; ​“monitor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly”, “monitoring elderly in home”, “in home monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly” ​and​ “relative monitoring elderly”. 
2.2. Problem context 
The purpose of researching the problem context was to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
validate the initial idea of the problem and to gain a more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
comprehensive understanding of both the main problem and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
related subproblems. 
2.2.1. An ageing society  
The world population is steadily becoming older. Globally, the
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
share of people aged 60 years or older has increased from 9,2
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
% in 1990 to 11,7 % in 2013 and is projected to reach 21,1 %
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
in 2050 (United Nations, 2013). Furthermore, the elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
population is also becoming older, as the life expectancy is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increasing. It is projected that the global share of people aged
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
80 years or older will increase from 14 % in 2013 to 19 % in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2050 (United Nations, 2013). 
 
In EU, the share of people aged 65 years or older is projected
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
to rise from 20 % in 2018 to 31 % in 2100, while the share of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the people aged 80 years or older is projected to nearly triple
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
from 6 % to 15 % in the same time span (Eurostat, 2019[A]). 
 
In Denmark, the amount of people aged 80-89 is expected to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increase by approximately 90 % from 2018 to 2040. Regarding
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the people aged 90 or above the amount is expected to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increase by around 150 % by 2040 compared to 2018 (KL,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2019[A]), see figure 4. 
 
Fig. 4 - Increase in the amount of old people from 2018 to 2025, 2030 and 
2040, source; (KL, 2019[A])  
 
At the same time the share of working-age people is predicted
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
to
decrease
in the future. Globally, the ratio between
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
working-age people (aged 15-64) and older people (aged 65
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
years or more) has declined steadily since 1950 and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decline is expected to continue in the future. In 2013, the ratio
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was 8 working-age persons for one elderly person and it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
12 
==PAGE:21==
 
expected to fall to 4 by 2050 (United Nations, 2013). This ratio
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
is even lower in the more developed regions of the world. For
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
instance, in the EU, the ratio in 2018 was slightly above 3
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
working-age persons for each elderly person and is expected
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to be fewer than 2 in 2100 (Eurostat, 2019[A]).  
  
If the retirement policies of today is retained at the same level,
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
these
demographic
developments
will
cause
significant
 
 
 
 
 
 
pressure on the labour force. Consequently, it is expected that
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
there will be less healthcare workers to support the increased
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aged population in the future. 
  
The big question is, if the elderly will experience a healthier
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
ageing in the future or if the morbidity will rise proportionally
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
with the life expectancy. If morbidity will increase with age, it
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
will cause an increasing pressure on the healthcare system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Chatterji et. al, 2015). 
2.2.2. Frailty in elderly people  
The likelihood of functional disability increases with age. A
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
survey made by the Congressional Budget Office [CBO] (2013)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in the USA, based on data from 2000-2010, demonstrate this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
correlation.
The
results
show
that
18
%
of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
community-dwelling
people
aged
65-74
experienced
 
 
 
 
 
functional limitations. For the age group 75-84 the results
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were 31 % and more than half (54 %) of the people aged 85 or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
above experienced functional limitations (CBO, 2013), see
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
figure 4. 
 
Fig. 4 - Overview of the increase in functional limitations with age, 
source;  (CBO, 2013) 
 
In the CBO (2013) study the functional limitations were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
derived from an assessment of the elderlies’ difficulty with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
performing
basic
activities
of
daily
living
(ADLs)
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
instrumental activities of daily living (IADLs). The basic ADLs
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are related to self-care and refer to the daily tasks that make
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
an
individual
live
independently
in
their
home
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Seniorliving.org, n.d.). The ADL’s included in the CBO study
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are the following; 
-
Bathing, dressing, eating, walking, transferring out of bed
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
or a chair​ and​ using the toilet. 
13 
==PAGE:22==
 
 
The IADLs are more complex and refer to the tasks that enable
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
an individual to live independently within a community
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Seniorliving.org, n.d.). The IADLs included in the CBO study
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are the following;  
-
Preparing
meals,
shopping,
using
the
telephone,
 
 
 
 
 
 
managing money ​and ​taking medications. 
 
The term ADL were originally developed as an index of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activities of daily living by Dr. Sidney Katz and his team at the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Benjamin Rose Hospital in Ohio in 1963 (Noelker & Browdie,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
2013). The index is also known as the Katz index and was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
original only based on the basic activities of daily living. Later
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Katz developed the instrumental activities of daily living as an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
add-on to functional assessment strategy (McCabe, 2019). The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
assessment of ADLs is perceived as one of the best and most
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used strategies for evaluating the functional status of elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
people (McCabe, 2019). 
2.2.3. Hospitalisation of elderly people 
It is commonly known that hospitalisation of elderly people
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
can lead to negative side effects, affecting their future health
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and well-being. Research have shown that many elderly people
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
develop new functional limitations after a hospitalisation (Wu
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
et al., 2000; Covinsky et al., 2003; Volpato et al., 2007; Gill et al.,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2010). The older and more frail the patients are, the more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
likely they are to experience functional decline and disabilities
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
after hospitalisation. Results of Volpato et al. (2007) show that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
almost 25 % of patients aged 85 years or above developed new
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
functional disabilities over the course of hospitalisation.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Observations made by Gill et al. (2010) show that up to 41.1 %
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of frail and hospitalised women aged 85 or more experienced a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
transition
from
no
disability
to
mild
disability
after
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospitalisation. 
 
King (2006) describes the hospital environment as “hazardous
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to older adults” (p. 270) and note that some of the main
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reasons for functional decline are complications derived from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the following aspects of hospitalisation; 
●
Enforced bed rest 
●
Hospital acquired infections 
●
Falls due to environmental hazards 
●
Medications 
●
Malnutrition 
 
This underlines the value of avoiding hospitalisation of elderly,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as it can be the root cause of new complications. 
2.2.4. Falls in elderly people  
The ageing process may affect vision, strength and balance,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which can lead to an increased risk of falling for adults aged 65
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
or
more
(National
Safety
Council,
n.d.).
According
to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
14 
==PAGE:23==
 
Sundhed.dk (2018), around every third person aged 65 or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
older will experience at least one fall incident yearly.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Furthermore, Bergen et al (2016) notes that falls account for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the leading cause of fatal and nonfatal injuries for older adults
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(aged 65 or more). Fleming & Brayne’s (2008) survey of fall
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
incidents among persons aged 90 or more, shows that persons
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
who fell were unable to get up again in 66 % of the reported
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fall cases. A key learning was that the inability to get up after a
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
fall increases with age, due to a decrease in muscle function. 
If an elderly person is living alone and is unable to get up by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
themselves, the consequences can be fatal. The time spent
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
lying on the floor waiting for help can cause additional harm,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
besides the damage caused by the fall. These additional
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
complications
can
be
pressure
sores,
carpet
burns,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dehydration, hypothermia (sub-cooling), pneumonia, and in
 
 
 
 
 
 
worst case death (Tinetti et al., 1993). The survey conducted
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
by Tinetti et al. (1993) shows that persons with an inability to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
get up, after a fall, had a greater chance of experiencing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
permanent functional decline (35 %), than persons who were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
able to get up by themselves (26 %).  
 
For a 12 week-period Gurley et al. (1996) followed the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
paramedics of San Francisco to carry out a study. They were
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
investigating the cases where a person was found helpless or
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
dead. Based on the study they defined a typical patient in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
following way; 
A typical patient was an 82-year-old white woman who
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was found by a neighbor during a routine check. She
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
was lying on the floor, soiled by dirt, feces, and urine,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and had garbled speech. She was estimated to have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
been incapacitated for more than 72 hours. She was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
admitted with decubitus ulcers and dehydration. After
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
21 days, she was discharged to a skilled-nursing facility.
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
(Gurley et al, 1996, p. 1711) 
The Gurley et al. (1996) survey found that the longer time the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
person had been lying helpless, the more cases of mortality,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospital admissions, and dischargement to facilities like
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
nursing homes were recorded.  
 
Because of the critical consequences a fall can cause, many
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
older adults experience the phenomenon ​fear of falling (FOF).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
According to Legters (2002), an average of 30 % or more of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
older adults, who have not experienced falling, will have some
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
degree of FOF. Regarding older adults who have experienced
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
falling, it is claimed that the average prevalence of FOF is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
doubled to 60 % or more. The experience of fear of falling can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have consequences for the wellbeing of the older adults.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Hadjistavropoulos et al (2011) identified that FOF can lead to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reduced
quality
of
life,
activity
restriction,
loss
of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
independence, and an increased risk of falling. ​A 2010 study
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
also found that anxiety about falling may lead to higher risk of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
falling. Results of the study show, that despite having a low
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
physiological fall risk, worrying about falling leads to more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
15 
==PAGE:24==
 
falls compared to elderly who does not worry as much
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Delbaera et al., 2010) 
 
The research done in this field substantiates the importance of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
developing and introducing tools that can both reduce the fear
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of falling and help detect if an elderly person is lying helpless
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
in their home, as it can have fatal consequences for the elderly.  
2.2.5. Elderly people living alone 
If an elderly person lives alone, many of the above-mentioned
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
challenges and complications can be worsened. Without a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
partner to provide support and awareness, it is hard to detect
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
and get timely help when accidents or decline in health occur.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
According to Eurostat (2019[B]), an increasing number of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
older people are living alone, especially elderly women. In
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2017, 40,4 % of older women (65+) were living alone in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
EU. In Denmark, the share of older women living alone was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more than 50 % in 2017, where the same number for older
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
men were only 35 %. An analysis made by KL (2019[B]) shows
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that elderly people (65+) are living alone in an average of 15
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
years after their partner has passed away. In 72 % of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
analysed cases, it is woman who lives the longest. The women
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
will in average live alone in 17 years before they pass away,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
where men will live alone in an average for 10,5 years. 
In Japan, the world's most rapidly aging society, elderly people
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
die alone and unnoticed in their apartments every day. It is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
estimated that around 4000 elderly die alone and unnoticed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
every year in Japan (Onishi, 2017). It is not uncommon for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their remains to go unnoticed for weeks, until for instance a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
neighbour start complaining about the smell. The problem has
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
gotten so big that an entire industry has emerged around
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cleaning up apartments after decomposing bodies (Kuo, 2015).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The case of japan is a horrifying example of what can happen
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
because of an ageing society lacking oversight of its elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
population. And it stresses the fact that elderly people living
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alone, compared to elderly people living with a spouse, have a
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
higher risk of taking their last breaths suffering and helpless. 
2.2.6. Relatives caring for an older adult 
The help and care that relatives provide for elderly family
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
members are substantial contributions. A relative involved in
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
care can entail a worthy and meaningful life for the elderly,
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
despite illness and physical decline. However, many relatives
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experience, that long-term care can be associated with high
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
social-, economic-, work- and health related costs (Ældre
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sagen, n.d.). 
According to a 2017 Voxmeter survey made for Ældre Sagen,
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
77 % of the Danish population provides or have been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
providing care for an elderly family member (Ældre sagen,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2017). From the same survey, the following insights about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives were concluded; 
16 
==PAGE:25==
 
●
The majority of the relatives are family, mostly children,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
but also children-in-law and grandchildren. 
●
Most relatives help an elderly living in a private home
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
or a sheltered housing unit for elderly. 
●
The relatives are mostly between 30-69 years old. 
  
In the same study, 95 % of the relatives agreed or partly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
agreed with the statement: “​it is natural for me to help” and 89
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
% agreed or partly agreed with the statement: ​“it is a pleasure
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
for me to help” (Ældre sagen, 2017). The majority of relatives
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
had positive experiences of providing help and care. However,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some relatives also agreed that it could be associated with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
negative experiences. The negative statements where the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
biggest part of the relatives agreed or partly agreed were the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
following;  
●
“I have to disregard myself and my personal needs” ​(35
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
% agreed or partly agreed) 
●
“I have feelings of guilt and bad conscience” (26 %
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
agreed or partly agreed) 
●
“It is burdening for me to provide help” ​(21 % agreed or
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
partly agreed) (Ældre sagen, 2017).  
  
In Denmark, there are expectations to what areas of the help
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and care the home-care services are responsible for, and what
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
areas the relatives are responsible for. According to Ældre
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sagen’s survey on relatives, 72 % agreed or partly agreed that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it is the relative’s job to help with “Support, oversight, finding
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
missing things, and providing motivation by visiting and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
telephone conversations”. On the other hand, 78 % disagreed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
or partly disagreed that it is the home care’s job to “perform
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
treatments of wounds, insert catheter, and give injections or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
other treatment” (Ældre sagen, 2017).  
 
 
17 
==PAGE:26==
 
2.3. Technological domain 
In this section, the technological domain related to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed monitoring system is explored. This is done by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigating the current status and history of the AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
research field. Also by reviewing research where sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technology is applied to monitor and detect critical deviations
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and events in the elderly’s home. 
2.3.1. Ambient assisted living 
Ambient assisted living (AAL) is an umbrella term used to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
categorize multiple disciplines, technologies, research, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
products. The AAL field aims at utilising information and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communication technologies (ICT) for creating smart solutions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
within healthcare and telehealth systems, with the purpose of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enabling elder people to age well at home (Belbachir, Drobics
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
& Marschitz, 2010; Al-Shaqi, Mourshed & Rezgui, 2016). AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
systems have shown great potential for solving many of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
societal challenges that comes with an ageing population,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
while on the same time improving the lives of elderly people
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Belbachir et al., 2010; Costin et al., 2011; Cahill et al., 2019).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The AAL field covers many topics, but one of the largest is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activity monitoring and emergency monitoring. Such systems
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
focus on event detection and behaviour understanding in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
context of the environment (Monekosso, Florez-Revuelta &
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Remagnino, 2015). Other AAL systems focus on human
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
machine interaction, e.g. using light prompts to train the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly in time orientation (Guillomía, Artigas, & Falcó, 2019).
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
In the context of this project, the focus has been on AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
systems of the former topic, namely systems concerned with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activity and emergency monitoring. 
 
In 2008, the AAL field gained traction in literature following
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the founding of the EU joint programme, ​AAL-programme​,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aimed to promote research and development in ICT-solutions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for ageing well (AAL-Programme, n.d.[A]). Prior to the EU
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
programme, many published articles used terms such as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Remote Activity Monitoring (RAM), Health smart homes, or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Telehealth monitoring to describe similar solutions, and some
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
still do. However, it seems that the AAL field has united many
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
researchers working within this domain and it has become the
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
preferred way of describing such solutions.  
 
 
 
18 
==PAGE:27==
 
AAL systems consist of sensor networks, computer networks
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and software applications interconnected to monitor, analyse,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interpret and exchange information. From an engineering
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design viewpoint, Memon et al. (2014) defines AAL systems as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
being: 
Multi-engineering systems consisting of components
 
 
 
 
 
and equipment from different engineering domains
 
 
 
 
 
 
spanning device manufacturing, energy consumption,
 
 
 
 
 
robotics, computing, and telecommunication. (Memon
 
 
 
 
 
et al., 2014, p. 4320) 
  
AAL systems are often thought to be used to provide insights
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about a patient’s well-being directly to a nurse or a doctor.
  
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
Therefore, AAL systems are also thought to be used as part of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
telemedicine practices to remotely provide health services to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
patients. A 2018 EU market study of telemedicine concludes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that the market potential of telemedicine is strong and is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expected to grow at a compound annual growth rate of 14% in
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the coming years. Furthermore, the EU study found evidence
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
suggesting that telemedicine is cost-effective in 73.3% of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cases covered by literature (European commission, 2018). Cost
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
effectiveness being one of the key drivers for AAL systems, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
current efforts within the field seems to create successful
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solutions. As outlined in section 2.2, there is an immense
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
socio-economic value in helping elderly stay independent and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in familiar surroundings for longer, as it may reduce the need
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
for external care. Something that the EU acted upon in 2008,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
when they under article 185 of the Treaty on the Functioning
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the European Union, established the joint AAL programme,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
involving 22 countries of the EU. The first joint AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
programme running from 2008 to 2013 was deemed a success,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
receiving
a
very
positive
evaluation
by
the
European
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
commission. The programme was renewed as a new AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
program running from 2014-2020 as part of the EU horizon
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2020. The new joint AAL programme is an applied Research,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Development,
and
Innovation
funding
programme
that
 
 
 
 
 
 
supports projects developing ICT solutions for ageing well. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
goals of the programme are to:  
1) Benefit the individual and their family.  
2) Benefit support and care systems across Europe.  
3) Benefit European economies.  
Furthermore, the programme underlines the importance of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
involving end-users in the development of AAL products,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stating that involvement of end-users is considered essential in
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
projects that are to be funded by the programme (AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Programme, n.d.[B]). 
  
In literature, AAL systems or parts of them are examined,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explored, tested, and/or developed in many ways. However, all
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
AAL systems have one thing in common; they all utilise some
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
form of sensor technology. In the following section, an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
overview of how different sensor technologies has been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applied in the context of AAL will be presented. 
19 
==PAGE:28==
 
2.3.2. Applied technologies in AAL monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
systems 
To understand what sensor technologies are applicable in a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
monitoring system, different AAL research articles were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reviewed. It was found that research in the AAL field differs in
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
focus. Some research focus on developing new methods,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
techniques, or technology solutions, while other research focus
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on testing systems or developing new ways to analyse and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interpret information. Additionally, it was found that there are
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
many
different
AAL
approaches
for
monitoring elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
home-dwelling people, differing in application purpose and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor types. From the AAL literature reviewed, 4 different
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
application categories were derived: 
 
1. Emergency
detection
-
​Systems
concerned
with
 
 
 
 
 
 
detecting critical events, such as falls or vocal distress
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
calls. 
 
2. Activity
monitoring
-
​Systems
concerned
with
 
 
 
 
 
 
detecting frailty or decline in health by monitoring and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
evaluating daily activities. 
 
3. Combined:
Emergency
&
Activity
monitoring
-
 
 
 
 
 
 
Systems concerned with both detecting critical events
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as well as monitoring daily activities. 
 
4. Health
monitoring
-
​Systems
concerned
with
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring specific vital parameters or functions (e.g.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ECG monitoring of a person's heart signature). 
 
Furthermore, it was found that the systems could be based on
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
two main sensor types or a combination of both: 
 
1. Wearable - sensors that are encapsulated in a wearable
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
device, a watch, a pendant, a phone, a shoe or a piece of
  
  
  
  
 
  
 
 
clothes etc.  
 
2. Ambient - sensors that are placed in the home of the
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly that do not need to be carried or worn by the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly. It could be sensors embedded in different
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
furniture or sensors simply attached to walls or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
furniture. 
 
An overview of the specific purposes, type of sensors and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applied sensor technologies for each category can be seen in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
table 1 on the following pages.  
 
20 
==PAGE:29==
 
Table 1: AAL-system application categories divided in purpose, type of sensors 
(A=Ambient and W=wearables) and applied sensor technologies.  
 
 
 
21 
==PAGE:30==
 
 
 
 
22 
==PAGE:31==
 
The most used sensor technologies and their application
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
opportunities is described in the following sections. 
Application of audio sensors 
Using microphones to capture and analyse audio events in a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
home setting, is a concept that many researchers have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explored. Ranging from studies concerned with using audio to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
detect activities of daily living (Siantikos et al., 2017; Vuegeri
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
et al., 2015; Giannakopoulos & Konstantopoulos, 2017; Vacher
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
et al., 2009) to studies focusing on fall and emergency
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
detection (Li, Ho & Popescu, 2012; Principi, et al., 2015;
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Vacher,
et
al.,2019).
In
general,
promising
results
are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
showcased
in the literature. In an experimental setup,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Siantikos et al. (2017) was able to distinguish between 5
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different
bathroom
activities
with
an
average
70%
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
classification performance. Interestingly, this was done with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
low cost hardware (Raspberry-PI) and with minimal training
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the machine learning algorithm. In the case of emergency
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
detection, Principi et al. (2015) developed a system able to
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
detect distress calls or abnormal acoustic events with the use
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of only a few microphones mounted on the ceiling. The system
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would
also
function as a hands-free home automation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
controller,
and
even
in
distress
situations
hands
free
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communication could be established through the system. With
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experimental results of 87.32% and 95.53% accuracy in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
emergency detection, respectively for a day setting and a night
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
setting, the systems shows very promising results.  
The robustness of solely using audio sensors is currently not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
good enough for real world application, as is evident in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
literature.
Especially
when
concerned
with
emergency
 
 
 
 
 
 
situations, it is crucial to have an extremely low margin of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
error. However, audio modality is a promising way to acquire
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
information that are not available through other classical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensors (Vacher et al., 2009). Thus, audio sensors can be very
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
valuable as complementary sensors in a sensor network. 
Application of PIR sensors 
A passive infrared (PIR) sensor is a highly useful motion
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
detector, due to its sensitivity to the Infrared Radiation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Changes (IRC) induced by human motion (Guan et al., 2019).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The PIR sensor and other similar infrared sensors are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
commonly known and widely used in applications such as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
automatic light switching or home intruder alarm systems. It
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
can be argued that PIR sensors also raise less privacy concerns,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as the technology is only able to detect changes in radiation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and not, for instance, capture images or sound. On top of this,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
PIR sensors come with a low price tag and a low operating
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
power consumption, making it a popular choice for AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applications.  
In the AAL field, PIR sensors have been used in a wide range of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
applications; detecting activity patterns, detecting frailty,
 
 
 
 
 
 
detecting sleep patterns, fall detection and in home localisation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(see table 1 for references). In the literature, PIR is shown to
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
be valuable in various configurations, whether it is a part of a
 
 
 
 
 
    
 
  
23 
==PAGE:32==
 
network of sensors or by itself. A 2018 experiment, involving
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
46 elderlies (60-91 years old), showcased impressive results in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their efforts of detecting frailty by use of PIR sensors. With a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
sensor installed in each room of the participants’ apartment
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(see fig. 5), they were able to detect sleep duration, intensity of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
movement, location occupancy of different rooms, and number
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of transitions between rooms. The study found that the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
domestic living pattern of an elderly, detected by PIR sensors
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and a door contact sensor, could successfully identify if an
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly was frail or robust. Even claiming to reach a 100% true
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
positive rate of frailty detection, if a 19% false positive rate
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
was tolerable (Goonawardene, Tan & Tan, 2018). Furthermore,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
Kaushik & Celler (2007) concludes that increasing the amount
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of PIR sensors in a room to 4 per room, can greatly increase
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
the sensitivity of the system, enabling detection of activities
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
such as reading a book or talking on the phone. One of the
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
advantages of PIR is that it can be configured to only require
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
very low computational time to be interpreted, meaning that a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
PIR system essentially can capture human activity in real time.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Something that a Chinese study from 2019 showcased, in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which they were able to detect 5 human activities (​walking,
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
fast walking, sitting, squatting, and falling​) in real time with an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
average correct classification rate of 91.6 % (Guan, et al.,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2019). 
Fig. 5 - PIR = Passive infrared sensor, DC = Door contact sensor & 
GW = Internet gateway. Source: (Goonawardene, Tan & Tan, 
2018). 
Application of video sensors 
Video
cameras
are
a
popular
choice
in
surveillance
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applications, and are known to be used for surveillance
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
purposes in many public places. However, due to advances in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
computer vision and image processing techniques, the use of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
video sensors has also found its place in the AAL field. Through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
computer vision it is possible to extract valuable information
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
from image or video sources, even in real time. A good example
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
24 
==PAGE:33==
 
of the capabilities of video based systems is presented in Yu et
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
al (2012) where they, in a simulated home environment, can
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
distinguish between the activities of ​walking, standing, sitting,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
lying, ​and ​falling​, with a fall detection rate of 97.08% and a
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
very low false positive detection rate of 0.8%.
Such high
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
accuracy suggests that video sensors could indeed be one of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the most robust ways to detect falls. Unfortunately, in home
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
video sensors are not perceived well by the end-users,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
especially if users are visible or recognizable in the AAL system
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Arning & Ziefle, 2015). In an attempt to alleviate privacy
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
issues when using camera sensors, Ricciuti, Spinsante & Gambi
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
(2018) developed a fall detection system using a Kinect camera
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
in a top view configuration. A Kinect camera only captures
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
depth information, hence the images it can produce cannot be
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
used to visually identify persons, see fig. 6 for an example.
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
With the Kinect setup, they could detect falls with an incredible
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
accuracy of 98.6%. However, a single Kinect sensor could only
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
cover an area of 8.25m​2​, meaning a high number of sensors
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
would have to be installed for the system to cover whole home.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
In conclusion, video sensors prove to be very useful in an AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
setting, but that does not come without raising concerns about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ethics and privacy. 
Fig. 6 - Human subject as captured by a kinect camera. 
Source: (Ricciuti, Spinsante & Gambi, 2018) 
Application of wearable sensors 
Wearable sensors come in many different variations. Today,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
most people walk around with an advanced wearable sensor in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their pocket every day, as smartphones and smartwatches are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
usually
equipped
with
accelerometers,
gyroscopes,
 
 
 
 
 
pedometers (step counter) and some even with ECG sensors.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Some researchers have investigated the opportunities of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
exploiting the use of smartphones and smartwatches in an AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
context. Sukreep et al (2019) proposes a fall and daily activity
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
recognition system based on smartphone and a smartwatch.
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
Through experiments they claim to be able to recognise 14
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
daily activities and falls with an accuracy of 99.19%. Similarly,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
25 
==PAGE:34==
 
Cheng et al. (2013) presents a framework utilising a wearable
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
accelerometer and surface electromyography to detect daily
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activities and falls with an accuracy of 98%. In general,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
wearable sensors seems to be a promising technology for AAL
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
applications.
However,
wearable
technology
demands
 
 
 
 
 
collaboration from the elderly, in the form of agreeing and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
remembering to wear the device, preferable both day and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
night, as well as changing or recharging its batteries. 
2.3.3. Hardware limitations 
AAL systems have to interpret information gathered by one or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more sensors, why they become a limiting factor for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
performance of the AAL system. McNaull et al. (2012) outlines
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and
discuss
factors
that
can
influence
the
quality
of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
information gathered by sensors in an AAL setting. They
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
highlight; ​intrinsic design flaws, component manufacturer,
 
 
 
 
 
 
calibration, application, and ​integrity as five influential factors
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that can impact the data quality. Furthermore, they discuss the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
importance of avoiding sensor error readings in an AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
healthcare setting, as it can lead to both false negative or false
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
positive events, creating potential dangerous situations for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
users relying on the system. In general, McNaull et al. (2012)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
underlines the importance of robustness in AAL systems, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
calls for more focus on accuracy and robustness in the design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of AAL systems for healthcare purposes. Other studies propose
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a solution to this topic, could be to incorporate multiple data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sources in the interpretation of specific events (Guettari et al.,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2010; Fernandez Arguedas et al., 2014; Deen, 2015). Deen
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(2015)
describes
the importance of sensor fusion and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
multimodality approaches as: 
 
The global performance of a sensing activity from a suite of
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
sensors should not be sensitive to individual sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
failures, so robust data fusion is very important in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
wireless sensor network. That is, the wireless sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
network must be much more capable than simple sum of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
capabilities of sensors. (Deen, 2015, p. 596) 
In the case of in home localization Guettari et al. (2010) shows
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that a multimodal approach can greatly increase the accuracy
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the system. By combining 2 modalities, an infrared sensor
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
system and a microphone based system, they could improve
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
the accuracy of the system by 54%. Furthermore, Fernandez
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Arguedas et al. (2014) proposes a sensor fusion technique,
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
providing a foundation for a scalable and dynamic multimodal
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
sensor network. Through experiments, they provide promising
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
results supporting the hypothesis that; higher amount of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
complementary
sensor
information
will
provide
higher
 
 
 
 
 
 
robustness and accuracy in a decision-making process. 
In conclusion, it is clear that more sensors in an AAL context
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
means higher robustness and accuracy. 
26 
==PAGE:35==
 
2.3.4. Existing AAL products for relatives 
In the academic literature found and described above, no
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
market available products were tested. The literature revolves
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
around testing and developing technologies for AAL systems in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experimental settings. However,
by searching youtube and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
google, it was possible to find three AAL products available on
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the market today. The products will briefly be described in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
following and more pictures of the specific products can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
seen in [A 2.2]. 
ELsa™ Activity sensing 
Elsa is developed by the finish company MariCare, who
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
describe themselves as the leading supplier of
sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technologies for the care sector (MariCare, n.d.). Elsa consist of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a smart stick, with motion sensors in each end, that can detect
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
if a fall occurs, and send an alarm to a preselected mobile.
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
Furthermore, the sticks can also collect behavioral data, based
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on the movement between rooms, that can help clarify
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
whether the elderly is eating frequently, sleeping enough and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
how often they visit the bathroom. Elsa also provides magnets
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for monitoring the use of for instance the refrigerator or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cupboards. The behavioral data is shown in an UI, where the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different activities of the elderly are logged throughout the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
day, see fig. 7. Only activities related to the rooms or items of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the house can be detected, such as sleeping, eating, using the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
bathroom and going outdoor. From the UI, different alarms can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be activated, which allow a rule-based interpretation of the
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
sensor data (MariCare, n.d.).
The expected price for a start
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
package containing a gateway, 3 sensor sticks and 2 magnets is
 
  
  
 
 
  
  
3.400 DKK with a monthly subscription fee on 320 DKK
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(CareWare, n.d.). 
 
 
Fig. 7 - A section of the interface provided by the Elsa activity sensing solution 
Source: (MariCare, n.d.) 
 
Elsa is mainly designed for and offered to the municipalities’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
homecare but could also be considered to be used by relatives.  
27 
==PAGE:36==
 
Aalborg municipality is currently testing the Elsa system in a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
pilot project. We have interviewed the chief consultant, Lars
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Nørh, who is running the pilot project, about their experience
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with the system. Lars explained that their incentive for testing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system is to evaluate whether the system can function as a
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
substitute for a physical home care visits, in the cases where
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the older citizens are asking for so called safety visits. He also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
notes that they believe that the system can make citizens afraid
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of falling more secure in their own home, which potentially can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reduce the risk of falling. Even though the pilot project is still
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
running, Lars mentions that they have been satisfied with the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
performance of the system, as it has alarmed the homecare
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
when the citizens have fallen, and they have only experienced a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
small amount of error calls. A more detailed description of Lars
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
Nøhr’s answers can be seen in [A 2.3]. The answers from the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interview will be used again in the discussion of the final
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system proposal in chapter 6.  
Canary care 
Canary care is a system developed by the british company with
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the same name, founded in 2013 (Canary Care, n.d.). The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
canary care system is mainly branded as a system for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
relatives, but it is also mentioned on the webpage that it can be
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
used in a homecare setting, where the homecare workers also
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
can get access to the monitored data. The system consist of a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
collection of wireless multipurpose sensors, that can be used
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as both motion and door sensors, a visitor card for home care
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
or other relatives and an interface, see fig. 8. The system is sold
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
in different packages, containing from 1 to 10 sensors.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Depending on the amount of sensors and their respective
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
placement, the user can gain insights in movement, bathroom
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
visits, sleep, visitors, room temperature, refrigerator use, use
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of water tap, or when the medicine cupboard is opened
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Canary Care, n.d.).  
 
 
Fig. 8 - The Canary Care system, Source: (Canary Care, n.d.) 
 
The Canary Care UI contains an overview of the elderly’s daily
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
routines and makes it possible to set rules in order to receive
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alarms if, for instance the elderly has not been in the kitchen in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the morning. Furthermore, it is possible to make the system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
28 
==PAGE:37==
 
push notifications that prompts the relative to check up. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
price of a package with 4 sensors is around 1.000 DKK with a
 
  
 
  
  
 
 
 
  
monthly subscription fee on around 270 DKK.  
TruSense  
TruSense is a product developed by the American company of
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the same name (TruSense, n.d.). TruSense is a collection of
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
connected home sensors, like contact sensors, motion sensor,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
water sensors, and GPS trackers, that can be purchased and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used in a range of different ways. The sensors are easily
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
installed on the walls of the home with adhesive tape, see fig. 9. 
 
Fig. 9 - The TruSense motion sensor attached to a wall with adhesive tape, 
Source: (TruSense, n.d.) 
 
Like Canary Care, TruSense is also a rule based system.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
TruSense proposes the following rules as options; “​too long in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
room”,
“away
from
home”,
“day
started/not
started”,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
“temperature out of range”, “vehicle out of area”, “water leak”
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and “​door open too long”​. TruSense is directly targeting the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
American family caregivers by offering their product to reduce
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the financial and emotional costs of caregiving (TruSense, n.d.)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
.  
The price of the platform is around 1.300 DKK with a monthly
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
subscription cost of around 330 DKK (Electronic360, n.d.). 
2.3.5. Data interpretation  
In an AAL system, vast amounts of sensor data are collected.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
To derive meaning from the raw data, it should be processed
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
and/or interpreted in some way. It is an integral part of an AAL
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
system to transform the data into a format that is valuable and
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
understandable for the user.  
  
In its essence AAL interpretation techniques are built on either
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mathematical-statistical processing or rule based processing of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the raw data. In some cases, AAL systems also use a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
combination of rule based and statistical processing to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interpret data. An example of this is the proposed AAL system
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
presented in Goonawardene, Tan & Tan (2018), where
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
multiple layers of interpretation are used. In their proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system, PIR sensor data was used to depict an elderly’s living
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
29 
==PAGE:38==
 
pattern, by extracting 5 rule based features from the raw data
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(e.g. sleep duration, location occupancy and frequency etc).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The interpreted 5 features were then further analysed and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interpreted by a machine learning algorithm, to measure the
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
frailty of the elderly.  
  
Another interpretation technique that could be applied in an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
AAL setting, is the statistical method called ​Statistical Process
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Control (SPC). Anhøj & Bjørn (2009) presents how SPC can be
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used in healthcare to analyse and understand variations in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
processes. They present how SPC effectively and easily can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used to distinguish between common cause variations and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
special cause variations in healthcare related processes. In
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relation to AAL monitoring, SPC could very well be a promising
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
solution to detecting special cause variations (significant
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviations) in behavioural data. 
  
Early Warning Scores (EWS) is another interesting data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interpretation practice. EWS is widely adopted in hospital
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
settings, to identify hospital patients that are likely to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deteriorate and need urgent attention. EWS systems exploit
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the fact that acute changes in vital parameters (heart rate,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
blood
pressure,
respiratory
rate
etc.)
occur
before
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deterioration. A set of simple algorithms is used to derive a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
score from the measured vital parameters, representing the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
patient's likelihood of deterioration. The EWS system thus
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
provides a window of opportunity for clinical interventions to
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
occur in an attempt to prevent serious patient deterioration
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Patel et al., 2018; Zografakis-Sfakianakis et al., 2018). Some
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
studies have explored the possibilities of bringing Early
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Warning Systems into pre-hospital settings. For instance, in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Anzanpour et al. (2016) an IoT-enabled wearable sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system is proposed to be used as an early warning system in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
an in-home setting. However, commercially available solutions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for bringing EWS out of hospital settings are yet to be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
developed. 
 
The above examples of interpretation methods could all be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
possible candidates to utilise in the proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system. However, as the focus of the thesis project has been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the
user needs and requirements for the UI and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor-system,
the
specific
interpretation
methods
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
algorithms have been viewed as a ​black box system​. A ​black box
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
is a system containing all the functions to convert certain
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
inputs into the desired outputs (Cross, 2008). In this case, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
inputs are AAL sensor data and the outputs are detection of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
critical events and deviations in behavioural trends, see fig. 10. 
 
Fig. 10 - AAL interpretation system expressed as a black box. 
30 
==PAGE:39==
 
 
For the purpose of the project, it was deemed feasible to build
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
algorithms, able to detect critical events and deviations in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
behavioral trends of a frail elderly person. 
The intention of expressing the interpretation system as a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
black box was to focus the design project on the users needs,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and not discouraging solutions based on the feasibility of data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interpretation. 
2.3.6. User acceptance of AAL systems 
Multiple studies cover the topic of acceptance of AAL solutions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
among the elder population (Mann et al., 2001; Arning & Ziefle,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
2015; Pol et al., 2016; Feldwieser et al., 2016; Mitchell et al.,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2018). An AAL system might be able to solve incredibly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
valuable tasks, but if it does so at the expense of the person
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
living with it, the value of the system quickly diminishes. To
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design a valuable and usable AAL system, it is important to
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigate what key factors influence the elderlies’ perception
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and accept of AAL solutions. 
  
In Arning & Ziefle (2015) the preferences and acceptance of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
video based health monitoring are evaluated in a range of
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
scenarios, based on 4 attributes. It is found that a key
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
acceptance driver is better emergency detection. Acceptance is
 
  
 
 
 
  
shown to be high for public health monitoring, but the overall
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
acceptance of video monitoring in private homes was very low.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
In a study conducted by Mann et al. (2001), it was found that
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
many of the participants showed enthusiasm towards the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
usefulness and importance of the in-home monitoring devices
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
they were shown. However, a majority of the participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
predominantly showed enthusiasm towards other people
 
 
 
 
 
 
using the devices, and not towards personal use, as they did
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not feel like they personally needed it. This shows some
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
anecdotal evidence of acceptance towards the technology, but
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not a desire to use it themselves.  
  
Another topic relevant to technology acceptance is cyber
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
security and privacy. Wilkowska & Ziefle (2012) finds that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
privacy
and
security
is important determinants in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
acceptance of assistive technology in a home environment.
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
Notable
findings
were
that
ailing
elderly
had
lower
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirements to the security and privacy standards, compared
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to healthy adults. In Pol et al. (2016), a study focusing on older
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
people's perspective on sensors in their home, it is concluded
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
that older people's sense of safety is more important to them
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
than their sense of privacy. In the study 11 elderly participants,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
living alone, had sensor networks consisting of contact
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensors, PIR sensors and a toilet flush sensor installed in their
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
homes. The clear majority of participants indicated that they
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
felt safer and that knowing they were kept an eye on, helped
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them stay more independent. However, in Mitchell et al.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(2018) 6 out of 30 study participants complained that the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
31 
==PAGE:40==
 
applied system was too disruptive and intrusive, rejecting the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
idea of sensors in their homes. 
 
CEN CWA (2020 forthcoming) depicts that a reduction of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
privacy is acceptable when the outcome is an increased
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
confidence that correct care is provided when needed. They
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
propose a model where the balance between the need for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
privacy and the need for care is dependent on the progression
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
of frailty and the progression of the technology, see fig. 11.  
 
 
Fig. 11 - ​Balance between privacy and care needs under the progression of
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technology and state of frailty, ​Source: ​(CEN CWA, 2020 forthcoming) 
 
CEN CWA (2020 forthcoming) state that the concrete balance
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
point depends on a complex of two contextual factors; 
1) Recognition that the care receiver’s need of care may
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
change over time (with progression of frailty). 
2) The advance of technology allows for ever deeper
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensing and pattern recognition. 
 
These findings support the claim that the concerns of privacy
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and
intrusiveness
are
individual
and
heavily
context
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dependant.
Hence,
it
is
important
to
investigate
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understand each elderly’s acceptance of the proposed system,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
before it is bought and implemented into their home.  
 
CEN CWA (2020 forthcoming) propose a practical, structured
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
guide
to
achieve
an
ethically
responsible
introduction,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
implementation
and
operation
of
sensor
monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
technologies in private homes of citizens in need of care. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
CWA guide propose that the caregiver should follow a risk
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
governance
model,
containing
an
adaption
of
the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
well-established process of informed consent. This should help
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
guarantee
that
the
monitoring
technologies
are
only
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
implemented in the extent accepted by the care receiver.  
2.4. Discussion 
Through the background investigation, it was found that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in-home monitoring systems of frail elderly people has been a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
research field for many years. The motivation and problem
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
32 
==PAGE:41==
 
context for conducting such research seems to have remained
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
similar. In Celler et al. (1995), a design of a remote monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
system able to monitor health status is proposed. They refer to
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
a rapidly ageing society and the rising healthcare costs of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly as motivation. Surprisingly, 25 years later, AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
researchers still talk about the same things and propose
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
modern versions of essentially the same system. How come
