AAL systems has been a research field for over 25 years, yet
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
there has been very little uptake of such systems in today's
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
society?  
In 1995, a limiting factor could be attributed to the ICT
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
infrastructure and the costs of sensor technologies. But, today
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ICT infrastructure has matured well beyond being a limiting
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
factor and consumers surround themselves with sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technologies everyday (Medagliani, Leguay, Duda, et al., 2014).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
So, what might the reason be, that AAL systems are not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
installed in all the homes of our elderly population? An
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explanation might be that AAL systems are still in the process
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of being accepted into society. In his book, design of everyday
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
things, Don Norman discusses this phenomenon:  
“Technology changes rapidly, but people and culture
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
change slowly. Change is therefore simultaneously
 
 
 
 
 
 
rapid and slow. It can take months to go from invention
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to product, but then decades - sometimes many decades
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
- for the product to get accepted.” (Norman, 2013, p.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
268).  
People and culture might very well be the last limiting factor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for the AAL system uptake in society, as the technology
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stopped being an actual limitation many years ago. 
While reviewing many articles for this thesis the thesis group
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
often noticed articles describing a technical AAL solution as
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
being “non-intrusive” without any evidence to back up the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
claim. Within the thesis group this was a concern often
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussed. A concern that was however shared by a 2016
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
review of the progress made within AAL for promoting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
independent living by the elderly. The review found that “Most
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the reviewed research appears to ignore and assume that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
users will accept the system in the way they design it.”
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Al-Shaqi, Mourshed & Rezgui, 2016, p. 17). This might be a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
result of many studies within the research field of AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
systems, focus on building systems to be used by healthcare
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
professionals and not the elderly adults themselves. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design requirements and goals of the system will in that case
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mainly be led by the healthcare professionals and not the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly who are supposed to live with the systems. Such a lack
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
of end-user focus can in turn lead to a mismatch between goals
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
of the AAL system and the end-user needs and expected gains,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ultimately impacting the end-users’ technology acceptance
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Chen & Chan, 2011). This calls for more user centered design
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
approaches and underlines the importance of including
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
end-users early in the design of AAL systems.  
 
33 
==PAGE:42==
 
2.5. Reflection 
Throughout the process of investigating the background
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
domains,
it
was
difficult
to
know
when
to
limit the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigation. The AAL field involves many topics and domains
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that the thesis team was not familiar with prior to starting the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project. A strong enthusiasm towards understanding the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
domains led to a decision of allocating more time, than was
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
previously expected, for this phase.
In retrospect, the time
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
seemed to be well spent, as it resulted in a solid foundation of
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
knowledge, of which the rest of the project was built upon.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Overall, this lead to very few moments of doubt, when
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
background related questions arose in the following phases of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the project. 
2.6. Conclusions 
The purpose of the background investigation was to gain
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
knowledge of the problem context and the technological
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
domain of the thesis. Based on 80 articles, the background
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
domains of the project have been investigated. First, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigation of the problem context validated assumptions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
regarding the nature of the problem; It is a fact that the society
 
 
 
 
 
    
 
 
 
 
is ageing and that it can cause enormous effects on the health
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
care system in the future. Also, central to the problem, ageing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
comes with an increased risk of functional limitations and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
falling. Both of which can have grave consequences for an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly person living alone.  
  
In the technological domain, the status and history of AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
systems were investigated. A table containing an overview of
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
research on AAL systems and applications were made and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
application possibilities of leading sensor technologies within
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the AAL field were presented. Lastly, the limitations of sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hardware, the possibilities for data interpretation, and the user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
acceptance of AAL systems were investigated and discussed. 
 
34 
==PAGE:43==
 
 
 
35 
==PAGE:44==
 
3. USER RESEARCH  
In this chapter, the process and results from the user research
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phase will be presented and discussed. The aim of this phase
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was to investigate the user needs and requirements through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
semi-structured interviews with both relatives and elderlies,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aiming to understand their stories, worries and perceptions of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the proposed AAL system.  
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
36 
==PAGE:45==
 
3.1. Methodology 
In this section, the specific methods, tools, and approaches
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applied in the user research phase are described. 
3.1.1. Semi-structured interviews  
The user research has been done through semi-structured
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
one-on-one interviews with both relatives to elderly family
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
members living alone and elderly people living alone. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
semi-structured approach was chosen in order to manage the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topics of the interviews but still allow flexibility in the process.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Thereby making room for questions that were not included in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the interview guide to be asked if new ideas arise throughout
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
the interview. In this project, a relatively clear research focus
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
was established before conducting the interviews. Therefore,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to investigate specific topics, it made sense to have some form
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
of structure in the interviews compared to a completely
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
unstructured interview. Semi-structured interviews are mainly
 
 
 
 
 
 
a qualitative method, focusing on the participants’ point of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
view. A focus which was actively sought to understand and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
empathize with users, in line with the design thinking
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
framework. Having some structure in the interviews also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ensured some form of cross comparability between the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interviews, making it easier to analyse afterwards.  
  
The interview procedure and interview style, have been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
inspired by the qualitative interviewing methods presented in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Bryman (2012). The interviewing procedure are further
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
described internally in section 3.2 and 3.3. 
3.1.2. Affinity diagram  
To analyse qualitative data from interviews, an affinity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
diagram method was applied. The affinity diagram is a great
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
tool for consolidating insights from different users to produce
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a comprehensive and shared picture of the user group (Beyer
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
& Holtzblatt, 1998). When using the method, verbal statements
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from the interviews are compared to each other and grouped
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
into themes. Doing so it is easy to identify specific relevant
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topics as well as shared and contrary opinions expressed by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the user group. 
3.1.3. Personas  
To
encapsulate
the
relative’s
different
needs,
multiple
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
personas were developed. Personas are fictitious characters
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
based on insights from the real users encountered. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
personas should create an image of the targeted user that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
convey key information and is memorable, engaging and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
actionable (Pruitt & Adlin, 2006). The personas worked as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
tangible representations of the target users and was used
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
actively in the design process, as design targets, in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
maintain a user-centered design process. 
37 
==PAGE:46==
 
3.2. User research - relatives  
In this section the user research of relatives are presented. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
main with the user research of the relatives, were to;  
1) Investigate their worries and difficulties related to taking
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
care of their elderly family member. 
2) Investigate their thoughts and perception of the concept of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in-home sensor monitoring.  
Recruiting process 
One of the recruitment criteria was that participants should be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
close relatives to an elderly person living alone, preferably
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aged 75 or more. An additional criteria was that they should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have some degree of worries about their family member’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
well-being and health status. The first step of the recruitment
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
process was to go for the “the low-hanging fruits”. This was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
done by asking our friends and family if they knew someone
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
who was taking care of and worrying about an elderly family
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
member. Through this strategy, we managed to get in contact
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with 6 different relatives, being professors from DTU, our own
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
family and friends, and our friends’ family.  
  
The second step of the process was to reach out to more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
potential participants, in the hope of recruiting someone that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
we did not have a relation to. To do this, a recruiting poster
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
was made [A 3.1] and shared in different Facebook groups and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on our own Facebook page. Even though the Facebook post
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was forwarded more than 20 times, only one participant was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
recruited from this strategy.  
Participants 
In total 7 relatives have been interviewed. 3 women and 4
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
men. The mean age of the participants was 57 (min: 43, max:
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
60). The participants’ relation to the elderly family member
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were mostly sons and daughters, only one was a nephew and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
one a son in law. Regarding the distance between the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants and the elderly, 3 of the participants lived far
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
away (300 km or more), 1 in a medium distance of (80km),
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
and 3 lived close to the elderly (25-5 km). The time span of
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which the participants had been worried ranged from 6 years
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
as the longest and 4 months as the shortest period. Data on the
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different participants can be seen in [A 3.2]. 
Interview procedure 
Preferably, the interviews were carried out face to face,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
however this was only possible in three of the interviews. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
rest of the interviews were handled via a phone call. This was
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
due to long distances between us and the participants. All
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interviews were audio recorded for later analysis. 
  
38 
==PAGE:47==
 
The interviews were conducted as semi-structured interviews
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
following a premade interview guide [A 3.3]. The interview
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
guide included both general questions about their worries and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
challenges,
and
concept
specific
questions,
about
their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thoughts and ideas of in home sensor monitoring. 
  
Related
to
their
worries
and challenges, the following
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questions were asked: 
●
What are your biggest worries about your elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relative’s wellbeing? 
●
What do you currently do to ease your mind? 
●
What is the most challenging parts of taking care of, and
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
helping your elderly relative? 
  
Questions related to the concept of in home monitoring were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
asked after a description of a concept. The most important
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
follow-up questions were the following; 
●
Could you be interested in using a concept like this? 
●
How could you imagine this concept could help you? 
●
What do you think your elderly family member may say
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to be monitored with sensors?  
●
What do you see as the biggest challenges with a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept like this? 
Analysis 
Most the data, extracted from the interviews with the relatives,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was qualitative. The answers to the question; “could you be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interested in using a concept like this?” was the only data that
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could be quantified with a yes or no answer. However, many of
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the participants did not answer with a quantifiable yes or no,
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
which made it challenging to quantify. The qualitative data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were
analysed
by
creating
an
affinity
diagram, where
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants’ quotes were explored, discussed, and organised
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
into groups, in order to identify recurring themes across the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different participant’s answers, see [A 3.4]. To collect the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
quotes for the affinity diagram, each recorded interview was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
listened through while valuable quotes were written down.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Quotes were deemed valuable if they expressed a thought, a
 
 
 
  
 
  
  
worry, or another emotion, thus small talk and out of scope
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
anecdotes were not included. Each quote was noted down on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
its own piece of paper, so it could be moved around to create
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
themes.  
3.2.1. Results 
In this section, the general themes derived from the affinity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
diagram are presented together with examples of quotes from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the interviews. The interviews were carried out in Danish, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
quotes presented below have been translated to English as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
directly as possible. The original Danish quotes can be seen in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
[A 3.5]. The results are divided in two sections, first section is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
about worries and challenges and the second section is about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
perceptions of the concept. 
39 
==PAGE:48==
 
Worries and challenges  
Falls are the biggest worry 
The most repeated “biggest worry” was that their elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
family member would fall and would not be able to get up by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
themself: 
“[biggest worry] It is that she falls in the middle of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
night and can’t get up again” - ​participant 1 (female)  
 
“(...) You often hear nasty stories about people who fall
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and lay helpless for a long time, maybe multiple days, I
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
think that is my concern” ​- participant 4 (male)  
 
A sign of life brings peace of mind  
The majority of the participants shared the feeling that getting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a sign of life from the elderly could put their mind at rest.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Among the participants, this sign of life was mainly achieved
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
through an answer to a phone call. Some of the participants
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
had permanent agreements about calling on a specific time
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
each day: 
“I call her every day at 9:30, so I can get a sign of life each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
  
 
 
day, sometimes it is just a short call and other times we
 
   
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
chat for 5 minutes​” - participant 4 (male) 
 
Another more alternative way of getting a sign of life, that one
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
of the participants shared, was to play wordfeud (a phone
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
game):  
“I play wordfeud with my mom for instance, and if there
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
is a day where she is not playing, then I would wonder
  
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
what was happening”​ - participant 5 (male) 
 
Action plan if the elderly does not answer the phone  
Another
theme
that
was
shared
among
many
of
the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants, was that they adopted specific strategies for how
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to act if the elderly did not answer the phone. If the elderly did
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
not answer, they would typically call again later. If there was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
still no answer, they would start to worry and check up
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
physically by coming by or arranging someone else to visit, if
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
they live far away: 
“[what if she doesn’t answer?] I call again, that is one of
  
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
the strategies, the other strategy is to ask my siblings if
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
they have been in contact with her (...), and then I would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
come by, I don’t live that far away”​ - participant 7 (male) 
 
Daily social contact is comforting 
In relation with the sign of life, one of the participants also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expressed that it brings peace of mind knowing that someone
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
has been visiting the elderly, and if this was not possible she
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
would be worried: 
40 
==PAGE:49==
 
“If we all lived far away, then it would be a big challenge
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
for me to know that no one was visiting her, then I would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
convince her that the homecare should come by daily” ​-
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
participant 6 (female)  
 
Planning and communication across family members 
Another theme that recurred among two different participants,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was the fact that taking care of the elderly involves planning
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and communication across many family members: 
“But if they are not home [the ones that usually come by
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
daily], then we will set it up, so we know that some of us is
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
calling or coming by” -​ participant 6 (female) 
 
“We have a whatsapp group chat that we use to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communicate about her, not with her, but around her” -
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
participant 7 (male) 
 
Challenging to live far away 
An interesting aspect mentioned by one of the participants was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that the most challenging part was the large distance between
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them, which made it impossible to come by and help with the
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
everyday life of the elderly: 
“[most challenging] It is that I can’t just pass by” ​-
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
Participant 1 
 
 
 
 
Challenging not to cross the elderly’s boundaries  
Another
challenge
that
was mentioned by one of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants was the difficulty of avoiding to cross any
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
boundaries of the elderly. And that it was challenging to avoid
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
interfering too much in the elderly’s own decisions related to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their everyday life.  
“[the most challenging thing about helping] Sometimes it
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
is actually not to cross the boundaries and not to take
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
charge and say what she has to do… because it is not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
always that we agree with her choices about what she
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
easily can manage herself” - ​participant 6 (female) 
 
Hard to assess the elderly’s well being as they refrain from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sharing their problems 
A theme that was recurring many times, was that it can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
extremely difficult for the relatives to figure out how the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderlies are feeling. This is a result of the elderly not sharing
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
their concerns, as they do not want to burden their children
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with their problems: 
“She was not feeling well and had not been feeling well
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for many days, but she did not feel like she had the right
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to tell any of us [her children] because we were busy, you
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
see” - ​participant 1 (female) 
Perception of the concept  
In total 6 out of the 7 participants expressed a mainly positive
 
  
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
attitude towards the concept of in home sensor monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
41 
==PAGE:50==
 
aimed at helping relatives to provide care and oversight. 5 of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
the participants expressed that they would be interested in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
using a system like the one proposed, however not without
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
precautions. In the following, themes related to the relatives’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
perception of the concept will be presented.  
 
It could put the mind at rest 
One theme that recurred related to the advantages of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept was that the system could make the relatives more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
calm, by avoiding some of the worries they have today: 
“Surely, it could be smart if I could see on an app that she
  
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was okay, even though she has not been calling that day,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
then I would not be so worried” - ​Participant 4 (male) 
 
It could provide more rest for the elderly  
Besides putting the relatives mind at rest, one of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants expressed that an extra benefit of the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system, was that it potentially could entail that her mom would
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
get more rest and relief: 
“You see, the fact that we didn’t have to come by could
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
give her some rest. You can also say that she would avoid
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that people are always calling and asking how she is” -
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
participant 6 (female) 
 
 
 
 
There are limits  
A theme that was recurring among the positive-disposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants, was that they would not accept all kinds of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring. Almost all of the participants expressed some kind
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of precaution with the level of monitoring: 
“Camera is gross right, you don’t want that installed in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
your mother’s home (...), but something that is more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
passive that measure toilet flush and water use, then you
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could still detect life signs” ​- participant 5 (male) 
 
“I would confine it [system] as much as possible, so it did
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
not feel that much like surveillance” - ​participant 1
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
(female) 
 
Challenging for the elderly to accept  
Another theme that emerged from the discussion about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept, was that many of the participants expressed concerns
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
whether the elderly would accept the proposed system:  
“ [about in home sensor monitoring] I don’t think she
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
[mom] would like it (...) I think that is the biggest
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
challenge” ​- Participant 6 (female) 
 
 
 
42 
==PAGE:51==
 
However, the majority of the participants expressed that they
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would only install and use the system if the elderly would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
accept it: 
“It is an important part that it is done with an acceptance
  
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
from the elderly in relation to the surveillance aspect and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensitive personal data” -​ participant 5 (male) 
 
Worried about replacing the social contact 
Related to the disadvantages and potential challenges of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept one theme recurred among 4 of the participants. The
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
participants expressed a concern about that the system could
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
replace the social contact the relatives are currently having
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with the elderly: 
“It could be that it could entail that you did not visit the
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly quite as often” ​- Participant 2 (male) 
 
“The argument for calling daily would not be there
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
anymore, that is maybe the downside to it” - ​Participant
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
4 (male) 
 
Critical of the usefulness of the system  
This is not a theme that recurred among many participants,
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
however it is still important to have in mind when designing
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system. One of the participants expressed many critical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thoughts about the usefulness of the concept. One of his
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concerns was that the proposed monitoring system would be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
an extra burden if it gave him too much data to interpret: 
“I don’t want to be flooded with data, that would not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
make sense, it would just create uncertainties, then we
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would have to interpret the data and we would be 4
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
people that would interpret it differently, then we had
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discuss that” ​- participant 7 (male) 
 
Another of his concerns was how data from sensors could help
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
him figure out how his mother was doing, because in his mind,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the best way to figure this out was to talk with her; 
“The way I know how she is, is to hear how she talks” -
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
Participant 7 (male) 
 
Requested insights 
When discussing the concept, many of the participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expressed specific wishes for insights they would like to gain
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about their elderly family member. The requested insights are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
listed below: 
●
Did they get out of bed? (3) 
●
Is there general activity in the home?  (3) 
●
Have they been drinking enough water? (2) 
●
Did they stay in the bathroom for too long? (1) 
●
Has the toilet been flushing as usual? (1) 
●
Have there been any social contact? (1) 
●
Did they fall and are they yelling for help? (1) 
●
Have there been a normal water or power usage? (1) 
●
Have the home care been there? (1) 
 
43 
==PAGE:52==
 
The numbers are indicating how many participants have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requested the specific insights. An interesting aspect is that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
even though most of the participants were most worried about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
falls, only one requested an insight about actual fall incidents.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
This can indicate that it is individually, what the relatives
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
perceive as valuable insights in order to put their mind at rest.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Or, it might indicate that some of the participants were in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
doubt of what sensor technologies are capable of.  
 
Requested features  
Besides the requested insights, extra features to the system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were also requested. One participants requested a kind of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
automated skype solution; 
“One thing that would be wonderful would be if I could
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
see her, but she should definitely not activate it herself” -
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
Participant 1 (female) 
 
Another participant requested something the elderly could
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activate themself, in order to lower the surveillance aspect in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system;  
“Maybe something she could activate herself (...) if
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
something was beeping every eight hour (...) then we
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could all see that she had turned it on or off” - participant
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
6 (female)  
3.2.2. Personas 
In order to encapsulate the insights gained from interviewing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the relatives, 3 persona cards were created. The personas
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
represent 3 different archetypes of relatives encountered in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the user research. The personas were given a look, a name, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
  
job, and a short background story, where their worries and
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
needs are described. The personas served as a foundation for
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
the next steps of the design process. By focusing on designing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solutions for the 3 personas it was ensured that design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
considerations and decisions were grounded in empathy for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the users. The persona cards are presented on the next 3 pages
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
in fig. 12, 13, and 14. 
 
44

==PAGE:55==
 
 
Fig. 14 - Persona 3 - Preben, the technology driven relative 
 
 
47 
==PAGE:56==
 
3.2.3. Discussion 
A limiting factor of the presented user research of the relatives,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
is the fact that only 7 relatives participated in the interviews.
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
The initial aim was to interview at least 10 relatives in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increase the amount of insights. However, it was challenging
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
and time consuming to recruit relatives, thus it was decided to
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
move forward with the insights gained from the 7 participants.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
This was done well knowing that during the interface design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phase the plan was to involve new relatives. Furthermore,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
including 10 participants would not change the fact, that it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
not possible to state anything statistically significant about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
market need or the acceptance towards in home monitoring,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
based on this research. But the qualitative insights gained from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the 7 participants was enough to identify 3 archetypes of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives that was further used as personas throughout the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design process. Had more interviews been conducted a 4th
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
archetype might have appeared, or the archetypes could have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
been defined in higher detail.  
3.2.4. Reflection 
An interesting aspect to reflect upon, is whether the interviews
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
were conducted face to face or over the phone. 4 of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants lived far away from us, thus it was decided to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conduct
the
interviews
over
the
phone.
However,
we
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experienced a critical difference between the interviews
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conducted face-to-face and the interviews conducted over the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phone. First of all, the face to face interviews were longer and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
felt more thorough relative to the phone interviews. This
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indicates that the face-to-face interviews have resulted in more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sharing of information, increasing the chance of receiving more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
valuable insights. When reflecting upon how it felt to conduct
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
the different forms of interviews, it is clear that the face-to-face
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
interviews were far better at making us empathize with the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
user. During face-to-face interviews, we could witness the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participant’s facial expressions and body language, thus
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enabling us to add another layer to the user statements.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Retrospectively, it would have been preferred to conduct the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phone interviews over a skype video call instead, enabling us
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
to see the face of the participants while interviewing them.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, the phone interviews made the user research
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
quicker and effective, as we were able to talk to some of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants on the same day as recruiting them. If we had
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
insisted on conducting a skype call, it would likely have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
required more planning from both sides. 
 
 
48 
==PAGE:57==
 
3.2.5. Conclusion 
In total 7 semi-structured interviews were conducted with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
target users. Generally, 6 out of the 7 participants, expressed
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
positive attitudes towards the proposed monitoring system, 5
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
of which expressed an interest in using a monitoring system.
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
From the analysis of the interviews 16 different themes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
emerged: 8 themes about the relatives’ worries and challenges
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
related to the care of the elderly and 8 themes about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives’ perceptions of the proposed monitoring system.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
From the analysis it was concluded that many of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants’ worries could be alleviated by simply receiving a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
sign of life from the elderly (e.g. a phone call). Also, most of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives explained that it was challenging to evaluate the well
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
being of the elderly. Furthermore, it was evident that many of
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
the participants had varying needs and boundaries for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed
monitoring
system,
underlining
the
need
for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
customizability. As a result of the analysis, 3 personas were
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
created, expressing the different needs and boundaries of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants; ​The anti surveillance relative, The worried career
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
woman ​and The technology driven relative​. The key learnings
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from the interviews with the relatives are summarised in table
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2.  
 
 
 
Table 2: Key learnings from the interviews with the relatives 
Key learnings - Interviews with relatives 
-
The participants’ biggest worry is if the elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
will fall and lay helpless for a long period of time. 
 
-
The participants find it hard to assess the elderly’s
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
well being, because the elderly often refrains from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sharing their problems. 
 
-
The participants expressed concerns about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
system replacing social contact. 
 
-
Many of the participants had varying needs and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
boundaries for the proposed monitoring system  
 
 
 
49 
==PAGE:58==
 
3.3. User research - elderly 
In this section, the process and results of the user research of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the elderly are presented. The main goals with the user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
research of the elderly, were to: 
1) investigate the elderlies’ worries about living alone as well
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as worries related to their health.  
2) investigate the elderlies’ perception of in home sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring. 
Recruiting process 
The
goal
was
to
recruit
participants
within
the
age
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
demographic of 75+ that were living alone at home (e.g. not in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a care home). The recruitment strategy was to produce a one
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
page recruitment poster [A 3.6] , and send it in an email to
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activity centres for elderly people in the municipality of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Copenhagen, Frederiksberg and Lyngby Taarbæk. In the emails
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
we were asking if they could post it on their walls or if we
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
could come by to recruit in person. The first cooperative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
respondent were the activity centre Bonderupgård located in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Vanløse. In Bonderupgård, many elderly people meet every
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
day to do exercises or to attend social events. The thesis group
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ended up spending 3 days recruiting and interviewing people
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
in Bonderupgård. The recruitment strategy was to first
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
establish a relationship with the people of the activity centre.
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
This
was
done
by
attending
lunch and having casual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conversations with the elderly people of the activity centre, see
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fig. 15. This helped the thesis team become familiar faces in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activity center. Ultimately, being a familiar face made it much
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
easier to recruit participants, as the people in the activity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
centre were happy to cooperate and help us recruit their peers
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
after being interviewed.  
 
 
Fig. 15 - Picture from the first day at the activity centre, getting to know the 
elderly by having casual conversations. 
Participants 
All the participants were elderly people visiting the activity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
centre,
Bonderupgård, in Vanløse in the municipality of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Copenhagen.
In
total 14 participants took part in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
50 
==PAGE:59==
 
interviews. The average age of the participants were 82 with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the youngest participant being 74 and the oldest 93. 6 (43%)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
of participants were male and 8 (57%) were female. 13 (93%)
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
of the participants were living alone and 1 (7%) lived with a
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
spouse. Also 5 (36%) of the participants owned a manual fall
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
alarm, in the form of a pendant. 
Interview procedure 
A series of face to face semi-structured interviews were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conducted. The interviews were prepared to have a higher
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
degree of structure, compared to the interviews conducted
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with the relatives. From personal experiences of talking to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly people (eg. our own grandparents), it is known that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some elderly can have short attention spans. Making the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interview guide more structured was done in an attempt to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
capitalise on their attention. Furthermore, effort was put into
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
avoiding the use of technical terms and words that could seem
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
foreign to the elderly. 3 concrete ways of explaining the idea of
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in home monitoring were rehearsed beforehand. Doing so, we
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were able to explain the concept with 3 different examples,
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
increasing the chance the participants would understand the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept. The participants were only available for interviews in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
limited time frames during the day, therefore interviews were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conducted over the course of 2 days. All interviews were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conducted with 1 interviewer and 1 notetaker and were audio
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
recorded for later analysis. 
 
As preparation for the interviews, an interview guide was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
made [A 3.7]. The interview guide included both general
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questions and specific questions, related to the concept of in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
home sensor monitoring. The general questions were mainly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about; 
●
Worries about living alone 
●
Worries about health  
●
Receiving help from home care and relatives  
●
Their relative’s worries about them 
 
After a description of the concept, the participants were asked
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questions
related
to
the
concept.
The
concept related
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questions were mainly about; 
●
Their thoughts  about a concept like this 
●
Their willingness to get a concept like this installed in
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
their own home 
●
Their perceived advantages and disadvantages of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept 
 
If a participant showed a positive attitude towards the concept,
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
they were asked follow up questions about what kind of data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would be too much to share with their relatives. This was done
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in order to investigate what type of data that would be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
perceived as too private to share.  
 
Finally, the participants were asked if they were afraid of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
falling or if they knew someone who had fallen and was not
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
51 
==PAGE:60==
 
able to get up, in order to clarify how big of a worry it is for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
   
 
 
elderly people. This question was asked in the end, in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
avoid priming the elderly with these thoughts throughout the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interview.  
Analysis 
From the interviews both quantitative data and qualitative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
data was extracted. A range of yes/no questions (e.g. “Do you
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
involve relatives in your worries?”) were used to quantify the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
answers from the interviews. An overview of the quantitative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
data extracted from the interviews can be seen in [A 3.8]. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
qualitative data was analysed by creating an affinity diagram
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with the same approach as described in the previous section.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
How the specific quotes were grouped into themes can be seen
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in [A 3.9]. 
3.3.1. Results 
In this section the results from the analysis of the interviews
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
will be presented. The results are divided into two sections,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
one about worries and one about perceptions of the concept.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The results are presented through both quantitative and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
qualitative data. General themes derived from the analysis are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented
together
with
examples
of
quotes from the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interviews. Because the interviews were carried out in danish,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the quotes displayed in this section has been translated to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
english as directly as possible. The original danish quotes can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be seen in [A 3.10]. 
Worries  
Only 3 out of 14 (21 %) articulated that living alone made
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them feel unsafe to some degree. Two different themes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expressed the reasons for a lower sense of security when living
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
alone; 
 
Unsafe because of a previous case of illness 
Some of the participants expressed that they started to feel
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
unsafe living alone after an experience of acute illness: 
“(...) I have just suffered from a brain haemorrhage, so
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
right now I can feel a little unsafe. (...) what happens if
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
something occurs?, because even though I have a phone, I
 
 
 
 
  
  
  
have to be able to activate it”  -​ participant 7 (male, 75) 
 
Unsafe due to experiences of crime  
Some of the participants explained that they felt insecure living
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alone because of previous experiences of crime, such as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
burglary: 
“Of course I am not happy about burglary and things like
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that, I have been burgled before” - participant 4 (female,
  
 
 
 
  
  
 
87) 
 
52 
==PAGE:61==
 
The rest ( 79 %) articulated that they have no explicit worries
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about living alone. Their reasoning for this could be divided
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
into two themes: 
 
Feeling safe due to frequent social contact 
One of the recurring answers was that they felt safe if they
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
experienced a frequent social contact, often in the form of their
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
neighbours, which made them belief that someone is keeping
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
an eye on them and will notice if something is wrong: 
“The residence [where he lives] has always been really
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
really good and we share concerns for each other (...), and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
if there is someone you have not seen in a day or two,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
then you ring at the door and ask how they are doing, to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
keep sure that you don’t lie on the floor and can’t get up
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and things like that” -​ participant 2 (male, 79) 
 
Aids can improve the sense of security 
Another theme that emerged, was that different aids, such as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fall alarms, wheelchairs and walkers also provided a sense of
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
security when living alone: 
“You see, I have this little alarm [a fall alarm pendant]
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that I can press if I get scared” - ​participant 4 (female,
  
 
   
 
  
  
 
87) 
 
Another interesting aspect to investigate, was the elderly’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
perception of their health status. Thus, all the participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were asked if they worried about their health and what their
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
biggest concerns related to their health was. 8 of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants (57 %) expressed that they had worries about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their health. Among these answers, two themes emerged, both
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about the kind of worries and the way of dealing with them. 
 
Worried about impairment of conditions 
Among the participants that were worried about their health, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
lot expressed fear and worries about impairment of their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conditions: 
“Now I have experienced this [brain haemorrhage], thus I
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
am often thinking about if it could happen again, and I
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
  
am afraid if I suddenly have a headache” - participant 7
 
   
 
  
  
  
(male, 75) 
 
Accepted impairment  
An interesting theme that emerged from the answers, was the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fact that the majority of the participants accepted their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conditions, which helped them ease their mind: 
“It [his biggest health related worry] is that I am
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
probably not going to be able to walk normally again,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
because of some neuritis [nerves infection] in my legs…
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
but ehh… you have to say; the time comes as it is and you
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
have to live with it, if it can’t be fixed according to the
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
experts, so be it” -​ Participant 5 (male, 78) 
 
Among the participants that did not worry about their health,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the following two themes emerged. 
 
 
 
53 
==PAGE:62==
 
Have been worried before  
Some of the participant did not have any worries about their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
health at the time they were interviews, but have been worried
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about their health at another point in life; 
“No not right now [worry about health] (...) I have an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
artificial knee that has been really swollen and I have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
been really worried, if I should have a new knee again or
 
 
   
 
  
 
 
 
 
if I should end in a wheelchair… but it has died down
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
again and now I don’t feel anything” - ​Participant 9
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
(female, 79) 
 
Living in the moment 
Many of the participants expressed that they do not spent time
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thinking or worrying about their health, because they just live
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in the moment and take one day at a time: 
“I don’t think about it [her health] that much, I take every
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
day as it comes…”- ​participant 11 (female, 93) 
 
“Ordinarily, I don’t walk around worrying, I try to deal
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
with it as best as I can” -​ participant 14 (female, 78) 
 
When asking the participants if they worried about falling, 5
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
(36 %) of them expressed that they were worried. From the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
answers to this question two themes emerged: 
 
Precautions alleviate worries 
Some of the participants who were worried about falling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expressed that being precautious when moving around, helped
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them limit their worries: 
“I have not fallen yet, I don’t hope so either, I use
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
something like walking sticks when I am out walking” ​-
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
participant 2 (male, 79) 
 
“I walk slowly and I am careful, because I know why I fell
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
  
 
that time a couple of years ago” - participant 11 (female,
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
93) 
 
Furthermore, the elderly also expressed worries about falling,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
if they have experienced falling before: 
“I am somehow afraid of falling, because I have friends
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
who have fallen and broken different things (...), and I
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
have also fallen myself, and I have broken my wrist, and
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
so on, thus I walk very carefully” ​participant 3 (female,
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
87) 
 
Not worried about falling, yet 
Some of the participants who stated that they did not worry
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about falling, were aware that it might become an issue in the
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
future:  
“Not quite at this moment [worried about falling], I don’t
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
have that problem… but it could possibly become a worry
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
in the future” ​- participant 1 (male, 79)  
 
Besides the elderly’s worries about living alone and their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
health, it was also interesting to investigate their relation to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
54 
==PAGE:63==
 
their relatives. This was investigated through asking the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants if their children were worried about their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
condition and how they feel about it. Related to these
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questions 6 (43 %) of the participants indicated that their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives (children in this case) worries about them. Four
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different themes emerged from this topic; 
 
Comfortable that relatives worry about them 
Some of the participants who indicated that their children
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
worry about them, expressed that it feels nice and that it make
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
them feel safe: 
“I know that they worry about me, we have a close
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relationship, sometimes they call just to ask how I feel,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
that makes me feel safe”​ - participant 14 (female, 78) 
 
“Yes, my son worry about me (...) sort of generally about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
everything, and I think that is very nice” ​- participant 8
 
  
 
  
 
  
  
(male, 88) 
 
Relatives interfere in their way of living  
Besides the positive feelings about relatives worrying, another
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more negative theme also emerged. Some of the participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expressed that the relatives interfere in their way of living and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decides things about their life: 
“I was sick and did not have strength enough, that was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
why my daughter made this set up, they [the family] felt
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more save knowing that I had a thing like that [fall
 
 
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
alarm]” -​ participant 12 (female, 90)  
 
Another participant expressed that it could be annoying when
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
his children interfere too much in his way of living: 
“It is of course pretty good that they ask, but sometimes
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
they have ideas for a lot of things, that I have already
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
thought about, so it can turn out to be slightly annoying”
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
- ​participant 7 (male, 75) 
 
No worries because I am tough and fit 
Among the participants who expressed that their relatives do
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not worry about them, it was often reasoned that it is because
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
   
 
their relatives think they are tough and fit: 
“No, quite the opposite, they brag about me and about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
what I do, and tell everyone that they can just get going
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
like their dad… but they are not worried about me” -
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
participant 1 (male, 79) 
 
“My daughter is not worried, she tells me that I will
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
become at least 100 years old” - Participant 11 (female,
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
93) 
 
 
 
55 
==PAGE:64==
 
Unwillingness to burden their children  
Some of the participants expressed a concern about burdening
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
their children, because they have already have enough on their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
plate with work and children: 
“(...) but there are also some things that I don’t think I can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
burden them with and talk to them about” - ​participant 2
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
(male, 79) 
Perceptions of the concept 
When explaining the idea of in home sensor monitoring to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
detect falls or other problems, the participants expressed both
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
negative and positive attitudes towards the concept. However,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the majority of the participants (9, 64 %) were positive about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the idea of in home monitoring. 5 (36 %) of the participants
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
were dismissive of the idea. An interesting insight was that all
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the male participants (6 out of 6) expressed a positive
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
attitude towards the idea, while the majority of the female
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants (5 out of 8) expressed a negative attitude towards
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
the concept. The remaining female participants (3) expressed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
positive thoughts. Among the participants with positive
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
attitudes towards in home monitoring, 4 general themes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
emerged. 
 
 
 
 
Noncritical acceptance  
Some of the participants demonstrated a beyond positive
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
attitude
towards
the
concept,
without
expressing
any
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
precautions. This was both regarding their thoughts and when
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
asked if they would like it to be installed in their own home: 
“It would not bother me at all, it really wouldn’t” -
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
Participant 2 (male, 79) 
 
“OK yes, you can just go ahead and install it [installing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
the sensors]” -​ Participant 10 (male, 80) 
 
Automation is an advantage  
When discussing the advantages and disadvantages about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept of in home monitoring, one of the shared advantages
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was that it is favourable that it is automatic. In this regard, the
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
concept was compared to the fall alarm they already know,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that you have to activate manually in order to get help: 
“(...) just imagine if you have an alarm like that [manual
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fall alarm], but have a fall and turn unconscious, well,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
then it won’t help. Then it is good with a system like that
  
 
 
   
 
  
 
 
 
[in home sensor monitoring]” ​- participant 14 (female,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
78) 
 
 
 
56 
==PAGE:65==
 
Offering a peace of mind  
Another theme about advantages that emerged, was that in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
home monitoring could offer a greater feeling of comfort and
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
make the elderly feel more safe in their home, ultimately giving
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them peace of mind: 
“The biggest advantage is the comfort it gives, sometimes
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
I imagine what if a disaster strikes? How long do I then
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
  
 
have to lie without help? I probably wouldn’t think as
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
much about it, with such a system [in home monitoring]”​-
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
participant 7 (male, 75) 
 
Personally, I don’t need it 
An interesting insight was, that even though the participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
liked the idea, it did not mean that they were willing to have it
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
installed in their own home. 2 of the participants that were
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
positive about the idea, expressed that they would not like to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
get it installed in their own home. They reasoned this with a
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
non-existing personal need: 
“It is a good idea, but I have good neighbours who keep
   
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
an eye on me, but I can imagine others, who are not as
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
lucky as me, for them it would be nice to have [in home
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring]” - ​Participant 14 (female, 78) 
 
 
 
 
 
Among the participants that expressed negative thoughts
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about in home monitoring, 3 different themes emerged. 
 
Against surveillance  
One
of
the
themes
that
were
repeated
among
the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
negative-disposed participants was that the concept was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
directly linked to surveillance, which the participants were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
against and thought would be too much: 
“I think we are about to turn into a surveillance society
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
and that is awful. I think the idea you propose is deeply
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
terrifying. You shouldn’t be under surveillance, then
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
people must come to visit you” - ​participant 9 (female,
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
79) 
  
“Apparently, I would be under surveillance, I don’t want
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
that, that is too much” - ​Participant 12 (female, 90) 
 
I already have a fall alarm  
Another
contention
mentioned
from
one
of
the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
negative-disposed participants, was that her manual fall alarm
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was enough: 
“I don’t think it is necessary, really. When I have my
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
alarm [fall alarm], then it is enough for me” - Participant
 
 
 
   
 
 
  
 
4 (female, 87) 
 
 
 
57 
==PAGE:66==
 
Too much to demand from my children 
A third reason some of the participant mentioned, was that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
they were not interested in burdening their relatives with a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
system like that: 
“Why should my daughter help me, when there are others
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
who can help? I would not like my daughter to be bound
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to something like that [the proposed monitoring system]”
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
-​ Participant 11 (female, 93) 
 
From the discussion about what and how much data the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly would share with their relatives,  4 themes emerged. 
 
No problem to share data with children 
The participants mainly expressed positive thoughts about

“Personally, I would be really comfortable with the fact
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that someone was watching, supposing I had a fall in the
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
 
 
bathroom” -​ participant 1 (male, 79) 
 
“It wouldn’t bother me, if my daughter was able to

I have nothing to hide 
Some of the participants could not see a problem with sharing

“Well, I have nothing to hide, really, so it does not matter

No problem with sleep monitoring 
The majority could not see a problem with sensors under the

“It [sensor] does not disturb my sleep, really, (...) I am
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
open for everything” - ​Participant 10 (male, 80). 
 
The line is drawn with personal hygiene  
For one of the participants, the monitoring of his hygiene was

“(...)The thing about if I am washing my hands or not... I
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
  
am not that sure I want people to know that about me...
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
how well my hygiene is (...) Well, that is too intrusive” -
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
  
participant 1 (male, 79) 
 
With some of the participants it was also discussed weather
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
they would like to watch and keep track of the data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
themselves. Some of them expressed that it would be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interesting: 
“I think it would be interesting to also watch the data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
myself” -​ Participant 8 (male, 88).  
 
 
 
58 
==PAGE:67==
 
And some of the participants expressed that they were already

“I already keep track on that [steps] today, I have such a
