day (...) it is something I care a lot about” - ​participant 1
 
   
  
  
 
  
  
(male, 79) 
3.3.2. Discussion 
An
interesting
finding
was
that
participants
who
had
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experienced acute illness or was in the process of dealing with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
long term illness, seemed to have a higher acceptance towards
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
the concept of in home monitoring. This echoes findings from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
other studies, e.g. Pol et al. (2016). Another interesting finding
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was that owning a manual fall alarm (pendant), seemed to
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
increase the participants sense of safety, meaning that a simple
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
system was enough to increase their sense of safety. For some
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants owning the pendant alarm was enough to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
disregard the idea of having sensors installed. However, other
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants that already owned a pendant alarm also showed
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
enthusiasm towards in home sensors. This could in part, be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
due to the fact, that they have already made peace with the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thought of needing such devices to help stay more independent
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and safe in their home.  
  
One concern about the results from the interviews, was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
whether the elderly had understood the concept of in home
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring correctly. The reason for this concern, was that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
during the interviews it was noticed that some participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thought the concept was an anti-theft alarm. Unfortunately,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
even after carefully explaining the concept again, with different
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
examples, some few participants were still commenting on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept as if it was a theft alarm. This made us doubt how
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
many of the elderly fully understood the system we proposed. 
 
 
59 
==PAGE:68==
 
3.3.3. Reflection 
When reflecting upon the process of interviewing the elderly,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
two aspects were found to be especially interesting. The first
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
being the focus of the elderly participant. Many of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interviews took longer time to conduct than expected, mainly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
since the participants often digressed from the subject at hand.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
It felt like many of the participants were happy to talk to us
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about their life and share stories about their children. Even
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
though listening to the stories gave some relevant insights into
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the elderly’s relationship with their relatives, the focus on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
actual topic and questions was lost in some of the cases. This
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experience showcased both the strengths and limitations of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
semi structured interviews. On one hand, it was valuable to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviate slightly from the topic, but on other accounts the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interviews lost value when deviating greatly from the topic.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Taking another approach to the interviews, for instance
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conducting the interviews using a survey, would likely have
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
kept participants more focused, thus speeding up the process.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
In that case, the results of the interviews would also change
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from qualitative to more quantitative. However, from a Design
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
Thinking perspective, it can be argued that qualitative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interviews would be the better choice to empathize with the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
users. 
  
The second aspect was related to the participants’ difficulty
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with understanding the concept, already discussed above. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept was only presented to the participants through an oral
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explanation. This taught us how difficult it can be to explain a
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
concept, when the technology behind is new and complex. In
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
retrospect, it would have been helpful to have drawings or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mock-ups to support the explanation. This might have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
eliminated some of the participants’ confusion. Another
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alternative considered, was to postpone the involvement of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly to a later stage of the process, where we would have a
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
better idea of what the concept could do, hence enabling us to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be clearer in the description of the system. However, involving
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the elderly early in the design phase was a conscious choice, as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
it was important to also empathize with them before making
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design choices. 
 
 
60 
==PAGE:69==
 
3.3.4. Conclusion 
In total, 14 semi structured interviews were conducted with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly people living alone. Through the interviews, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants’ worries related to living alone and related to their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
health
were
investigated.
Furthermore, the participants’
 
 
 
 
 
 
perception of a proposed remote monitoring system was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigated. From the analysis of the interviews 14 themes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about the elderlies’ worries and 11 themes about their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
perception of the concept emerged. In general, it can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concluded that 9 of the elderly expressed a positive attitude
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
towards the concept of in home monitoring and 5 of them
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expressed a dismissive attitude towards the concept. This
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
supports the importance of an established agreement or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
informed consent between the elderly and the relative, before
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
buying and implementing the system. The key learnings from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the interviews with the elderly is summarised in table 3. 
 
 
 
 
Table 3: Key learnings from the interviews with the elderly 
Key learnings - Interviews with elderly 
-
9 out of the 14 participants expressed that they 
found the proposed monitoring system valuable 
 
-
Most of the participants who was worried about 
their health or had experienced falling expressed a 
personal need for the system. 
 
-
The participants perceived the increased feeling of 
safety as the main advantage of the proposed 
system. 
  
-
The automation of the system was perceived as a 
strong advantage (compared to an emergency 
pendant). 
 
-
The majority of the participants did not see any 
problem in sharing data about their daily activities 
with their relatives (except personal hygiene) 
 
 
 
 
 
61 
==PAGE:70==
 
3.4. Stakeholder map 
Besides affecting the lives of the main users, the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system can also affect other external stakeholders.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
In order to investigate this, a list of stakeholders, that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
potentially
could
be
affected,
was
brainstormed.
The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
brainstorm was based on learnings from the background
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigation
and
the
user
research.
The
identified
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stakeholders were divided in 3 categories: 
 
1. Primary
stakeholders​:
​The
users
and
direct
 
 
 
 
 
 
influencers on the daily use of the proposed AAL
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system. 
 
2. Secondary
stakeholders​:
​Non users that can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
involved in, come in direct contact with, or be directly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
affected by the use of the proposed AAL system.  
 
3. Contextual stakeholders​: ​Non users that might have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interest in the effects of the proposed system. 
 
The identified stakeholders were mapped in an onion style
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stakeholder map (see fig. 16). The stakeholder map was used
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
within the thesis team to discuss potential conflicts between
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stakeholders
that
could
occur
when
implementing
the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed system. From the discussion, 4 potential points of
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
conflict were identified (marked with a lightning symbol in fig.
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
16).  
 
 
Fig. 16 - Stakeholder map - The lightning symbol indicates where possible 
stakeholder conflicts might occur (original content). 
 
62 
==PAGE:71==
 
The identified points of conflict will be described in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
following. 
 
Stakeholder conflict: Healthcare professionals 
The proposed monitoring system might end up being used by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives as a tool to provide “evidence” for a health-related
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
concern. A relative could for instance show data from the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system to the elderly person’s general practitioner,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to convince the practitioner of an arbitrary health concern.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, a practitioner might have to refuse to take the data
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the AAL system seriously, to avoid clouding his professional
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
judgement. In a general sense, healthcare professionals must
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
rely on robust data sources to base their decisions on, e.g. data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
coming from medical equipment that is calibrated after certain
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
standards, or what they can see and physically feel on a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
patient. That is not to say that healthcare professionals do not
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
listen to relatives’ statements, but rather that they should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
distance themselves from using 3rd party data sources when
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
diagnosing patients. In some cases, this might lead to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relative being frustrated that a healthcare professional does
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
not take the data seriously. In the same context, the healthcare
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
professional might be frustrated having to explain why the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
data is not of use. 
  
Stakeholder conflict: Emergency response 
A potential effect of the proposed system, could be an increase
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in false alarm calls to the emergency response service. In a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
situation where the proposed system detects a critical event or
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
a critical deviation it might prompt the relative to call
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
emergency services immediately. However, the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
system might have detected a critical event that does not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
necessarily require the assistance of the emergency services.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The conflict therefore arises, if the proposed system will
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prompt users to overreact and misuse emergency services. 
  
Stakeholder conflict: Home care 
From the interviews conducted with relatives, it was evident
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
that some relatives could imagine using the proposed system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to monitor home care visits. Some relatives stated that, if given
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
the opportunity, they would like to know if the home care had
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
shown up on time and if the home care had shown up on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
weekends or not. However, this could in turn be interpreted by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the home care workers as being spied on, thus creating an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
unpleasant
work
environment.
Potential
conflicts
could
 
 
 
 
 
 
therefore arise between the relative and the home care
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
workers and the municipality.  
  
Stakeholder conflict: Neighbours 
A stakeholder that might be unwillingly involved in the care
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and oversight of the elderly, is the neighbour. Certain
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
situations might arise, where the system detects a deviation or
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
a critical event, but the relative is too far away to physically
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
check up on the elderly. In a case like that, the relative might
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
think to call the neighbour of the elderly and ask them to check
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
63 
==PAGE:72==
 
up on the elderly. The neighbours will probably feel morally
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
obligated to do so, in case it was an emergency. The conflict
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
arises, if this becomes an often-recurring situation. This could
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be the case, if for instance, the relatives were highly sensitive
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to small deviations that they can infer from the data
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communicated through the system.  
  
These identified conflicts were taken into consideration when
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
moving forward with the system definition and UI design. It
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
was important to develop a design that would not foster these
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
conflicts, but rather a design that could prevent the occurrence
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
of these. 
 
 
 
3.5. Conclusion   
In this chapter, the process and results from the user research
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phase have been presented and discussed. The user needs and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirements have been investigated through semi-structured
 
 
 
 
 
 
interviews with 7 relatives and 14 elderly people living alone,
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aiming to understand their stories, worries and perceptions of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the proposed monitoring system. The results of the interviews
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are presented and concluded in section 3.2 and 3.3. In general,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it was outlined that both the relatives and the elderly face
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different worries and thus have different needs for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed system. In conclusion, it was found to be important
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
that the user should be able to personalise the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system to suit their individual needs. Lastly, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stakeholder map was made to discuss potential stakeholder
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conflicts that could arise with the implementation of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed
system. 4 potential points for conflicts were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
identified. 
 
 
64 
==PAGE:73==
 
4. DEFINITION OF THE 
SENSOR-SYSTEM  
In this chapter, the process of defining the sensor-system of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the proposed monitoring system is presented. The aim of this
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
phase
was
to
explore and define how the underlying
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor-system should work, in order to establish a baseline for
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
the UI design.  
 
 
 
 
65 
==PAGE:74==
 
4.1. Methodology 
In this section, the specific methods, tools, and approaches
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applied in this phase are described. 
4.1.1. Morphological Chart 
Morphological analysis and creation of a morphological chart
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
is a method to explore and identify alternative design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solutions. Generally, the method is used to widen the search
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for new solutions. The morphological chart is constructed of a
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
list of features or functions, essential for to the product,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
combined with a list of different means to how the functions
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
might be achieved (Nigel Cross, 2008). The method was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
adapted and applied in this phase, to systematically explore
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and define specifications of the underlying sensor system. 
4.1.2. User scenario 
In this chapter, user stories were used to explore how the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different personas would use the defined sensor-system.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Writing user scenarios is a narrative method that can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applied to explain and concretize ideas, guide design, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communicate value (Geven et al, 2006). Through storytelling,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the perspective and context of the personas’ actions are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explored, including their emotions and motivations for acting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as they do (Quesenbery & Brooks, 2010). 
4.2. System breakdown  
The concept definition was initiated with a system breakdown,
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
with the aim of understanding and defining what different
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
parts
the
monitoring
system
consists
of.
The
system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
breakdown was combined with a goal breakdown, in order to
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
understand how the different parts of the system relate to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
achievement of a main vision. The breakdown was carried out
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with a breakdown structure approach, where the main vision
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and system is branched out to goals and subsystems, see fig.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
17. 
 
Fig. 17 - Top level of system- and goal breakdown structure  
66 
==PAGE:75==
 
As seen on figure 17, the overall system vision, providing peace
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of mind to relatives of frail elderly people living alone, was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used as the top level of the breakdown structure. From the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
vision, the main system was derived. The main system was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
then broken down into 3 goals describing what the system is
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
trying to achieve at its core. From each of these goals a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
subsystem was derived; 
  
In home sensor-system: This subsystem contains functions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and attributes related to achieving the goal of monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
behavioural trends and events. This being for instance all the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hardware needed for monitoring and the installation and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
running of those.  
  
Data interpretation system: This subsystem contains the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
functions related to processing and interpreting the data from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the sensors, to detect deviations in behavioural trends and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
critical events. 
  
UI for relatives: This subsystem is the communicative part. It
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
should enable the relatives to receive information about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviations in the behavioural trends and critical events.  
  
The three subsystems are closely interrelated, which means
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that the design choices made within one subsystem can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
influence the others. For instance, the design of the in-home
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor-system and the data interpretation system have a great
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
influence on how the UI for relatives can be designed. Thus, to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have a baseline for designing the UI for the relatives, it was
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
found important to first establish a conceptual definition of the
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
in-home sensor-system and the data interpretation system. 
4.3. Initial user requirements 
To scope the concept definition, a range of initial user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirements for the monitoring system were defined. As a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
base for this, the previous collected data were reviewed, by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
going through the background investigation and the results
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from the user research. This approach helped establish a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
common understanding of the user needs, and made sure that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the initial requirements were driven by previous learnings.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The monitoring system was at this point still in a conceptual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
stage, thus the initial requirements was on a high level of
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
abstraction. The requirements that were defined is presented
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
in [A 4.1]. 
 
 
67 
==PAGE:76==
 
4.4. Concept definition  
To define the concept of the sensor-system, both the ​in-home
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor-system and the ​data interpretation system ​were broken
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
further down into a range of attributes; 
  
In home sensor-system 
●
Sensor installation 
●
Data output 
●
Sensor type 
●
Power source 
●
Connectivity 
  
Data interpretation system 
●
Processing location 
●
Interpretation approach 
  
The different attributes represent possible specifications for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the subsystems. To expand the solution space, different ideas
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for each attribute were brainstormed, see fig. 18. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
inspiration for the ideas mainly came from the background
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigation
of
the
technological
domain
and
wishes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expressed by the relatives in the user research. With the initial
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept requirements as a point of reference, a concept was
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
defined based on the list of ideas. The chosen concept
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
specifications have been highlighted with a black border in
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
figure 18. 
 
 
 
68 
==PAGE:77==
 
 
 
Fig. 18 - System brainstorming and definition (black frame= chosen 
specifications) 
 
 
69 
==PAGE:78==
 
The chosen system definition revolves around enabling the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
users to customize the system, both regarding the desired
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
functionality
and
level
of
obtrusiveness.
By supporting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
multiple sensor types (PIR, contact & microphone), the users
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
can pick, choose, and combine the sensors that enable them to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fulfil their needs. In the following, reflections about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
specification choices are presented. 
 
In home sensor-system 
Sensor installation 
In terms of sensor installation, it was important that the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
installation would be simple and easy, and not require house
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reconstruction work. The idea is that sensors will be installed
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
on surfaces with either screw mounts or adhesives. While in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some cases, sensors could also just be placed in a less
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
permanent configuration, for instance on a shelf. Wearable
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
sensors were decided not to be included. The main reason for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
this is that it requires collaboration from the elderly to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
function, in the form of remembering to wear it and turn it on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
etc. Some relatives expressed concerns related to the elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
having to manually interact with parts of the system, thinking
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it was too hard for the elderly to properly operate such things.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, it could possibly still be valuable to include
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
wearables as a supporting unit, but not as a stand-alone part of
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
the system. 
  
Data output 
Multiple different data outputs can be gathered from the same
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
types of sensors, as it has been shown in literature and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussed in the background research. 7 out of the 8
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
considered data outputs were chosen to be included in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept. All the features included has been specifically
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requested by the relatives interviewed in the user research.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, it was prominent that not all users would have the
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
need for the same outputs. Therefore, the idea of implementing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
many
different
data
outputs,
was
to
increase
the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
configurability of the concept. This demand a function in the
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
UI, where the output can be selected or deselected. Some of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
data outputs, simply rely on how and where sensors will be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
installed in the physical space of the house. Monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
resource usage (power and water) was an idea requested in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the user research, as a way of monitoring the daily living in a
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
very unobtrusive and passive way. An interesting idea that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could find its use, but not without the compromise of replacing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
water gauges and electrical meters. It was deemed not suitable
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
for this concept, as it would require advanced installation and
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
possibly
construction
work
to
be
done
in
the house.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Nevertheless,
it
would
be
interesting
to
include
such
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technology in the construction of future houses. 
  
 
 
70 
==PAGE:79==
 
Sensor type 
In total, 9 sensor types were considered for the concept. The
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor types ​PIR and ​Microphone were both chosen to be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
included in the concept. Both ​PIR ​and ​Microphone sensors can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be categorised as multipurpose AAL sensors, as they can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used in a range of different sensing activities. Including both
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensors, makes it possible to greatly increase the robustness of
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system, as well as giving the user a choice between 2
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
different multipurpose sensors. Additionally, contact sensors
 
 
 
 
 
 
were also chosen to be included, as they give the possibility to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitor opening of doors, cupboards, and refrigerators etc. in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a simple way. These sensors have more specific purposes as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
well as less depth of information, but are easier to interpret.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The main reasons for including the 3 mentioned sensors in the
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
concept was: 
1) To enable high customizability with low amounts of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensors. 
2) To accommodate different user preferences. 
3) To increase robustness through multimodality. 
  
Power source 
Powering the sensors in the home, was chosen to be done with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
batteries. The use of batteries eases the setup and installation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and removes the need for installing new wires in the home.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, using battery power entails that the batteries will
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have to be recharged or changed after prolonged use. Hence,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the power consumption of the sensors should strive to be as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
low as possible. Another design consideration would be to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have the UI indicate the power level of the various sensors
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
installed, making it easy for the relatives to predict when to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
change or recharge the batteries. 
 
Connectivity  
Both Wi-Fi and 4G was chosen as connectivity options. 4G
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enables the system to be installed in a home, with no Wi-Fi
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
connection, thus easing the installation process. However, if
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
the system could only function with 4G, users that already
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
subscribe to a Wi-Fi service, would wonder why the system
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could not utilise this. Keeping both options available should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not, to the thesis groups knowledge, be a complex or costly
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
affair. 
 
Data interpretation system 
Processing location 
A pressing issue in monitoring humans is privacy and security
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
concerns. Because of this, it was considered the best choice to
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
process the data locally. By processing raw data locally, it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
possible to avoid transferring sensitive raw data over the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
internet. As an example, when using a microphone sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
installed on the bathroom to detect bathroom usage (e.g.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
flushing, bathing, brushing teeth), the audio recordings would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have to either be sent to the cloud for processing or be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
processed locally. Processing such recordings locally, would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mean that the actual recordings would never be stored
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
71 
==PAGE:80==
 
anywhere, and that it would never leave the privacy of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
user’s home. The results of the interpretation of the recordings
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
will eventually leave the house, to be sent to the UI in a simple
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
form, e.g. in a simple text string like “toilet flushed”. 
  
Interpretation approach 
Interpreting the data collected by the sensors is an important
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
task. The data collected has to be interpreted into something
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
useful and understandable for the users. As found in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
background
investigation,
this
is
usually
done
with
a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
rule-based approach or a mathematical-statistical approach. It
 
 
  
 
  
was decided, that the system should use both approaches. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reason for implementing a statistical data interpretation is to
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
enable the relatives to receive a deduced status indication of
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
the behavioural trends. It is assumed, that this could make it
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
easier for the relatives to figure out when the health or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
wellbeing of the elderly is declining. Furthermore, all the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
commercial monitoring systems targeted relatives were found
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to only be based on rule-based data interpretation. Thus, it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
possible to differentiate by introducing a statistical approach.  
The reason for implementing a rule-based interpretation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
approach, is to provide the possibility to create customized
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
if-then alarms, based on specific individual needs. For instance,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
if the elderly has been away from home for more than 5 hours
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
or if they have not left the bedroom before 12. 
 
 
72 
==PAGE:81==
 
 
 
73 
==PAGE:82==
 
4.4.1. Storyboard  
Based on the definition of the sensor-system, a simplified
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
storyboard was made, visualising and describing the overall
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
use of the proposed monitoring system, see fig. 19. Overall, 5
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
major steps of interaction were identified; 
  
Step 1: This is the initial step of interaction, where both the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly and the relative agree on the implementation of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system. In this step, it is important to establish informed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
consent from the elderly. 
  
Step 2:
At this step the elderly and the relative discuss and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decide in collaboration what type and number of sensors
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
should be purchased. 
  
Step 3: The purchased sensors are easily installed in the home.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The elderly should also be involved in the installation process.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
This way the elderly will be aware of where the sensors are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
installed. 
  
Step 4: After installing the sensors in the home, the relative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
can now make the desired configurations of the system in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
UI. 
  
Step 5: Now the sensors are installed and the system is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
configured, the relative can view the status of behavioural
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
trends and receive alarms if critical deviation or events occur.  
  
If the condition of the elderly worsens, the user might want to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
upgrade or change the system setup, eg. install more sensors to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enable real time fall detection. In that case step 1-4 would be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
repeated. 
74 
==PAGE:83==
 
Fig. 19 - simplified storyboard, describing the overall use of the proposed 
monitoring system  
 
 
75 
==PAGE:84==
 
4.5. User scenarios  
The proposed monitoring system was further explored and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
described through the creation of 3 user scenarios. The user
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
scenarios were based on the 3 user personas (Thomas, Marie
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
and Preben), that was defined during the user research phase.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Each scenario is described through a user story. The user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stories describe the motivation, goals, and use context of each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
persona. The scenarios focus on what the users want, rather
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
than how to achieve it. Meaning that the scenarios attempt to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
describe the experience and not details about the installation,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
setup or interface. 
  
The floorplan visualisations (fig. 20 - 22) of the 3 scenarios
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
also effectively display the customisable aspect of the concept.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Furthermore, the user scenarios helped explore the variances
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of use that the UI should be compatible with. The scenarios are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented on the next pages.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
76 
==PAGE:85==
 
4.5.1. Scenario 1 - The least invasive 
Thomas’ biggest worry is that his mom will fall and hurt
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
herself and end up lying on the floor helpless, for many hours
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
or even days before anyone notice her. However, he is not a big
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
fan of surveillance, so he just wants to have a simple setup,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
with only one sensor, that can monitor the general activity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
level and detect behavioural trends to give him an indication of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sign of life. It is important to him that the system is not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
perceived by his mom as intrusive, thus he does not want to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
place the sensor in the bedroom or the bathroom. His mother
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
spends most of the time in the living room during the day, so
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
he chooses to place the sensor here. When going about his
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
busy day, Thomas can through an UI on his phone quickly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
check if his mom has gotten up from bed and entered the living
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
room as she usually does. He sends a text to his mom saying
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
good morning, and even if she doesn't answer within the hour,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
his mind is at ease, as he knows she has moved around the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
house as she normally does. See figure 20 for a visualisation of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
the sensor setup. 
 
Fig. 20- ​Scenario 1​ floor plan. Green dot = PIR sensor 
 
 
77 
==PAGE:86==
 
4.5.2. Scenario 2 - A Sensor in every room 
Marie is worried that her mother will fall and hurt herself
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
while being in the garden, so she chooses to buy a contact
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
sensor to install on the door. In this way, she can set up the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system to push an alarm, if her mom goes outside and don’t
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
return after a critical period of time. Marie is also worried that
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
her mother is sitting still all day long, so she also decides to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
buy and install a PIR sensor in each room. With the PIR
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensors, Marie can track her mother’s daily level of activity,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and observe how the activity evolves over time. This helps
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Marie to know when her mom is in a period of slump, sitting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
still or sleeping all day. Marie can then plan to come by and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
take her mother out for walks. See figure 21 for a visualisation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
of the sensor setup. 
 
 
Fig. 21 - ​Scenario 2​ floor plan. Green dot = PIR sensor & blue dot = contact 
sensor. 
  
78 
==PAGE:87==
 
 
4.5.3. Scenario 3 - Real time fall detection &
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
activity sensing 
Preben is worried that his father will fall and be unable to get
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
up without help. He is afraid that his father will lay on the floor
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for a long time before he or somebody else will notice that
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
something is wrong. Because of this, he wants to install enough
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensors to enable real time fall detection. Therefore, Preben
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decides to buy and install two PIR sensors and one Microphone
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in each room.
Preben also wants to have an indication of his
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
father's well-being, especially if his health seems to be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
declining. He will therefore also use the sensors to evaluate
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
movement between rooms, sleeping pattern, intensity of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
movement, activity recognition etc. Furthermore, he would like
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to be reassured when the homecare has visited his father, so
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
he also installs a contact sensor on the door. Preben just wants
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
what is best for his father, and puts his faith in the technology
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to greatly improve both his own life and his father’s. See figure
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
22 for a visualisation of the sensor setup. 
 
 
 
Fig. 22 - ​Scenario 3​ floor plan. Green = PIR, blue = contact sensor, yellow = 
Microphone. 
 
 
79 
==PAGE:88==
 
4.6. Discussion and reflection 
The process of defining the underlying sensor-system was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
carried out, to have a baseline for designing the UI. By doing so
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some of the choices made, when defining the in-home
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor-system would end up dictating design possibilities of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the interface. Another option was to design the UI first, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
then let the UI design set the requirements for the underlying
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor system. However, it was decided that to promote a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
more realistic UI design, it made sense to define the underlying
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensors
system
first.
Furthermore,
creating
a
specific
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
definition of the underlying sensor system, made it easier to
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
communicate the concept idea to the users when carrying out
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
user tests. Having a specific definition to reference, made the
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept more realistic, compared to referring to some sort of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
magic sensors that could monitor whatever the users wanted.  
  
In the end, it can be argued that by letting a realistic definition
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
of the underlying sensor system limit the UI design, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solution space became narrower. A narrow solution space is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not necessarily a good thing, when the goal is to find
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
innovative solutions. However, in the case of this project, it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
helped us to set up the right boundaries, giving us more time to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
refine the UI design. 
  
4.7. Conclusion 
In this phase, the underlying sensor-system of the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring
system
has
been
defined.
First,
the
main
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system was broken down into three subsystems; ​in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
home sensor-system, data interpretation system ​and UI for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives. The functions of the in-home sensor-system and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
data interpretation system were further explored and defined.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The result of this phase was a specific conceptual idea of how
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
the sensor-system behind the UI should work. Based on this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conceptual idea, the use of the monitoring system was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigated through a simplified storyboard and 3 different
 
  
 
 
  
 
user scenarios based on the 3 personas created in the user
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
research. 
 
 
 
80 
==PAGE:89==
 
5. UI DESIGN 
In this chapter the UI design phase will be presented. The goal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of this phase was to develop a design proposal for an UI that
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
can
fulfill
the
essential
user
needs
and
requirements.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Throughout the chapter an iterative UX design process,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
containing a scoping and 3 design loops, will be presented.
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Concluding, a final UI design proposal will be presented.  
 
 
 
 
 
81 
==PAGE:90==
 
5.1. Methodology 
In this section, the specific methods, tools, and approaches
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applied in the UI design phase are described. 
5.1.1. Lean UX approach 
As explained in the intro chapter, the UI design process has
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
been inspired by the lean UX design approach proposed by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Laura Klein (2013). Mainly, the process has been driven by the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
idea of the “lean loop”, where ideas and hypothesis are quickly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
validated through simple user tests. Through the UI design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phase, the UI has been developed through 3 specific design
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
loops, see fig. 23.  
 
In each loop, ideas were build into interactive prototypes. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototypes were subsequently tested with users, in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
gather new learnings and new ideas. In Loop 1, the prefered
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
type and level of information were investigated. This was done
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
by developing and testing 3 different low-fidelity (Lo-fi)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dashboards. In loop 2, the setup and configuration flow was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
developed and tested, also with a lo-fi prototype. Learnings
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
from loop 1 and 2 were then compiled and used in loop 3,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
where a refined dashboard design were developed and tested
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
through a high-fidelity (hi-fi) prototype. Lastly, the learnings
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
from loop 3 were used to develop the final UI design proposal. 
 
fig. 23 - The UI design process consisting of 3 loops 
 
In this chapter, the 3 loops will be presented through three
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
subsections; 
●
What​: ​Describing the ideas and prototype to be tested. 
●
How​: ​Describing how the ideas are tested. 
●
Outcome​: ​Describing the learnings from the tests. 
82 
==PAGE:91==
 
5.1.2. User story mapping 
The idea behind user story mapping (USM) is to tell the user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
story through different levels, in order to gain a shared
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understanding of the product, why you are building it and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
what it should contain (Patton, 2009). Furthermore, it can help
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
you keep the user and their needs in mind in order to ensure a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
user driven design process (Patton, 2009). In this project, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
USM was used to scope the UI design while also functioning as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a changeable recipe for designing the different views of the UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
throughout the process.  
5.1.3. The 10 usability heuristics 
The 10 usability heuristics defined by Jakob Nielsen are 10
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
general principles and rules of thumbs for interaction design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Nielsen, 1994). The heuristics were used as general guidelines
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to keep in mind when designing the UI and was used as a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
checklist continuously throughout the design process. The 10
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
heuristics are as follows:  
1. Visibility of system status  
2. Match between system and the real world 
3. User control and freedoms 
4. Consistency and standards 
5. Error prevention 
6. Recognition rather than recall 
7. Flexibility and efficiency of use  
8. Aesthetic and minimalist design  
9. Help users recognize, diagnose and recover from errors 
10.Help and documentation  
 
The idea behind all the heuristics are further described in an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
article on Jakob Nielsen’s webpage (Nielsen, 2005). 
5.1.4. Interactive prototypes 
In order to test and validate the different iterations of UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
designs, interactive prototypes were developed. The idea
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
behind the interactive prototypes is to simulate a real UI in
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
order to get as close to a real product as possible. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interactive prototypes have been made in Adobe XD, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
programme
specially
developed
to make interactive UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototypes. In Adobe XD you can sketch each view of the UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and then link them to each other, so it is possible for the user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
to click through the prototype, mimicking a real product,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
without having to develop backend software. 
5.1.5. Validation 
The most important part of an iterative UX design process is to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
validate your ideas fast and learn from them in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
continuously improve the product as you learn more and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more. When it comes to detecting usability issues, Nielsen
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(2000) have shown that it is possible to identify around 85%
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
83 
==PAGE:92==
 
of usability issues with only five participants. Hence Nielsen
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
states that;  
The best results comes from testing no more than 5
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
users and running as many small tests as you can afford.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Nielsen, 2000) 
Therefore, in the case of this thesis it was strived to do as many
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
tests as possible, with around 4-5 users participating in each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
test. 
5.1.6. Think aloud testing 
“Thinking aloud may be the single most valuable usability
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
engineering method” - Jakob Nielsen writes in his textbook
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
usability engineering (1993). Think aloud testing involves the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants sharing their thoughts while simultaneously
 
 
 
 
 
 
navigating through the user interface. This allows for designers
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to easily understand how users view the prototype and makes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it easy to identify misconceptions. Think aloud testing provides
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
qualitative feedback on the system that is being tested, as the
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
users share their experience. The biggest strength of the think
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aloud test is that it is possible to see what the participants are
 
  
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
doing while simultaneously hearing their reasons for why they
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are doing so (Nielsen, 1993). For that reason, when conducting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
think aloud tests in this thesis, the participants screen actions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in the prototype was recorded and synchronized with their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
verbal comments. However, a limitation of the think aloud
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
method is that thinking aloud can seem strange and unnatural,
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thus affecting the way participants interact with the prototype.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
For instance participants might work their way slower through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system.  
5.1.7. Prototype fidelity  
During the 3 loops of testing, the prototypes used for testing
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
differed in fidelity. The fidelity of a prototype refers to how it
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
communicates the realism of the final product. The fidelity of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototypes generally vary in the areas of ​Visual design, Content
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and ​Interactivity (Babich, 2017). For instance the prototype
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used in the first loop had low interactivity, and low visual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design. This was done intentionally to put focus on the content
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the prototype rather than the visual design of the prototype.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Whereas the prototype used in loop 3 was a high fidelity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
prototype, looking and functioning almost like it was a finished
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
product. The idea of testing with a high fidelity prototype is to
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
force the participants to be critical and give more honest
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
feedback, even on the small details. The hypothesis is that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
when a prototype looks like a real product, the users will be
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
more likely to behave naturally and expect more of it, when
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
testing it. Furthermore if a prototype has low fidelity, it is
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
expected that the users are less critical, as they clearly can see
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that it is an unfinished product. On top of that, low fidelity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototypes often require more imagination from the user,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which can end up limiting the outcome of the tests. 
 
84 
==PAGE:93==
 
The prototype strategy used has been to start low and then
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increase the fidelity in every loop. One of the main reasons for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
starting with low fidelity prototypes was to be able to build
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
multiple
prototype
variations
in
a
short
time
frame.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Afterwards as key design choices had been made, the fidelity of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the prototype could be increased. Ultimately ending up with a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
close to finished design proposal for an UI. 
 
 
85 
==PAGE:94==
 
5.2. Scoping of the UI design 
In order to explore and define what the UI should contain, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
small scoping phase was carried out.  
5.2.1. User story mapping 
In order to discuss and define what functions the UI should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
contain, a user story map was made, see fig. 24. Here the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
overall user goals were defined and broken down into a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sequence of activities the user should go through in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fulfil their goals. The activities were then broken down into
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
specific tasks the user should have the possibility to perform
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
via the UI. Throughout the UI design process the USM worked
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as a shared recipe for building the specific views and the flow
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the UI. Furthermore, the USM was used as a tool for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussing and defining what features should be a part of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
minimum viable product (MVP). The idea behind a MVP is to
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
only focus on the core value of the product when developing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and testing the product. Tasks that were not assumed to be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
important for the MVP was moved down to the 2. edition slice
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the USM.  
 
86 
==PAGE:95==
 
 
Fig. 24 - User story map 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
87 
==PAGE:96==
 
5.2.2. User flow 
In order to get a better idea of the functions and views the UI
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
should contain and how it should be designed, the user flow
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
were investigated. The usage of the UI could be divided in 3
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
different user flows representing different moments and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reasons for interaction, see fig. 25.  
 
1. First time use 
This user flow is only meant to be happening the first time of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
user and is a prerequisite before the system can function. This
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
scenario can be broken into 2 separate use flows, setup (install
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
sensors) and configuration (choose insights, define alarms).  
 
2. Daily use  
This scenario is where everything have been set up and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
configured and the system is running automatically. Here the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
use flow is triggered by a thought/concern about the elderly
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
family member or a notification/alarm. Either the user checks
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
the UI to get peace of mind or the user receives an alarm if an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
alarm is triggered.  
 
3. Change of setup and reconfiguration  
This use scenario only happens after the initial setup and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
configuration, if a user wants to change the configuration after
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system have been used for a while or if the user wants to
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
add a new sensor. 
 
Fig. 25 - User flow: the usage of the interface can be  divided in 3 different 
flows 
 
88 
==PAGE:97==
 
Even though the first time use flow and reconfiguration flow is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
viewed as two separate flows, many of the features would be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the same. This means that the part of the UI that should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
support the first time use should be reused for the UI part that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
should support the reconfiguration flow.  
5.3. Loop 1: Type and level of 
information  
The focus of this loop was first of all to investigate what kinds
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of data and what level of information the user would prefer to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
see in the main dashboard view of the UI. Furthermore, the test
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the dashboards designed in this loop was also used to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
validate
the
concept
of
the
underlying sensor system,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
previously defined in chapter 4. 
5.3.1. What  
The main dashboard view is the first thing the relative will see
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
when they open the app. It should contain only the most
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relevant information, that can give the relatives piece of mind.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
In order to investigate what level and what type of information
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the relatives prefer, 3 different variations of dashboards were
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
developed. The level and type of information displayed on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dashboards was varied according to the needs of the 3 user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
personas and their respective use scenarios. A low-fidelity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
semi-interactive prototype was made for all three dashboard
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
variations. Keeping the fidelity low was done both in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
emphasize the variations in content and not variations in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aesthetics and to minimise the time used at developing the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototype.  
 
The dashboard variations are described in the following
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
section together with pictures of their respective prototypes.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
An overview of all the views from each dashboard prototype
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
can be seen in [A 5.1] 
 
89 
==PAGE:98==
 
Dashboard 1 - Low level of detail 
This dashboard was designed to align with the needs of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
persona 1, Thomas, who did not want to feel like he was spying
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on his mother. This design takes into account that in scenario
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
1, there would only be one PIR sensor installed in the living
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
room. The dashboard is kept simple and the immediate
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communication of knowledge is done by showing a thumbs up
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
or thumbs down symbol. If a deviation is detected the symbols
 
 
 
   
  
 
 
 
would change accordingly, see fig. 26.  
 
Fig. 26 - Main view for both a good (left) and a bad (right) scenario

is knowing that his mother has gotten out of bed and have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
started the day. Therefore, this dashboard is designed to push
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
a notification when the first activity of the day is detected in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the living room, see fig. 27. Furthermore the dashboard
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
contains a simple history view of the daily activity level during
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the last week. This can give Thomas an idea of the general
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activity level of his mother.  
 
 
Fig. 27 - Notification (left) and history view (right)

5.3.2. How  
The user’s perceptions of the different dashboard variations
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were investigated through tests and interviews with possible
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
end users. The dashboards were tested with 4 participants in
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
total, 2 previously interviewed and 2 new. The participants
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
were all relatives to a home dwelling elderly person living
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
alone, making them the target users. 
  
The participants were asked to click through one concept at a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
time and comment on what they liked and did not like. In the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
end they were asked to choose which dashboard variation they
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
liked the best and explain why. Furthermore, the illustrations
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the 3 scenarios (from p. 77-79) were printed out. These
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were shown after the test of the dashboards in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
facilitate a discussion about the number of sensors and their
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
placement, respective to the amount of detail available on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dashboard. A test guide [A 5.2] was used to conduct the tests,
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in order to structure the tests in the same way. 
  
Pictures of the wireframes from the dashboards were printed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
out and brought to the tests. The printouts were used to take
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
notes on during the interviews and tests in order to document
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the results in an easy way. 
 
5.3.3. Outcome  
In this section the most interesting outcomes of the test will be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented. All of the tests results are presented in details in [A
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
5.3].  
  
All of the participants showed interest in using one or more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
variations of the dashboard which validated that the system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
idea had developed in the right direction. 
​All participants liked the simple thumbs up/down indicator in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dashboard 1, as it provided an easy to understand and instant
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indication of whether the status is positive or negative. In this
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
regard
most
participants
found
the
​activity
score
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
well-being indicator in dashboard 2 and 3 to be confusing and
 
 
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
ambiguous. 2 participants asked “what does it mean? is it good
  
 
 
 
  
   
 
or bad?” when looking at the activity score displayed on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dashboard 2. They suggested that such a score or indication
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
could be written in words instead of numbers to make it less
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
ambiguous.  
 
The indication of the current activity e.g. ​home / not home /
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
sleeping shown in dashboard 2, was perceived as being highly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
valuable by all participants. When asked about the importance
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the time stamp on the indication, all participants agreed that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it was especially important to keep the timestamp.  
 
93 
==PAGE:102==
 
3 out of 4 participants perceived the activity log in dashboard
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3 to be valuable. The log was useful for them to gain more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
context to the status of the elderly. The participants also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mentioned
that
the
activity
log
could
encourage
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communication, a sort of ​ticket to talk​. For instance, a relative
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
getting encouraged to call the elderly in order to hear about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the elderly’s day, if the elderly for instance, had been away
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from home for a longer period that day. 
  
The history view incorporated in all 3 dashboard variations
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
was perceived as valuable. The participants liked the idea of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
being able to see a development over time, especially over
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
longer periods. However, some participants questioned how
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system was able to calculate the activity score to make the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
graphs. Also it was noted that the graphs would be easier to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
read if they were labelled with weekdays instead of dates.  
  
3 out of 4 participants perceived the monitoring of toilet use as
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
well as monitoring of sleep to be unimportant or too intrusive.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Drastic changes in toilet use for instance, could indicate illness,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and thus be valuable in the sense of discovering illness in its
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
early development. However, one of the participants were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concerned that monitoring sleep and toilet use, would only
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
make her even more worried or confused. Another participant
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explained that he did not expect to be able to interpret the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sleep and toilet use data into something useful.  
Based on the outcome from the user tests in this loop an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
updated user requirement list for the proposed monitoring

1 are summarized in table 4.

are
not

5.4. Loop 2: Setup and configuration 
From the user research and loop 1 a key learning was that the
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
relatives strongly preferred an AAL system they could install
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
themselves. In order to fulfil this need, it is important that the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
interface can guide the user easily through the setup and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
installation of the system. Furthermore, it is important that the
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
interface contains an intuitive configuration flow, giving the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
user the experience of being able to tailor the system to fit
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their specific needs.  
5.4.1. What 
In this loop, a prototype of the setup and configuration flow
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was developed and evaluated. This was done to investigate
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
how the first time use interface should be designed in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ensure an easy and intuitive setup and configuration of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system.  
 
First step in developing the setup and configuration flow was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to explore what different wireframes were needed to ensure
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
an intuitive flow for the user. This was done through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
brainstorming different wireframes on post its and connecting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them to each other to create different flows, see fig. 32. 
 
 
Fig. 32 - Section of the post it flow, showing the section where sensors 
should be installed and paired.  
Developing the first wireframes on post its helped open up the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solution space and facilitated valuable discussions, that led to a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
more common understanding of how the prototype should be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
developed. The final post it flow can be seen in [A 5.5].  
 
An interactive prototype was made based on the final post it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
flow. The flow was divided in two, a setup flow and a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
configuration flow. The different steps of each flow is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
illustrated and categorised as ​physical or ​in App in figure 33
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
below. All the steps that are categorised as in App are actions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
95 
==PAGE:104==
 
to do in the App, where all the physical steps are actions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
related to the house or the sensors.  
 
Fig. 33 - Illustration of the prototype flow for the setup and configuration. 
 
The prototype was made with a low-fidelity visual design with
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
blue and white colours in order to keep the focus on the flow
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and not the visual design. A part of the prototype can be seen
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in fig. 34, showing two views from the set up flow, and in fig.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
35, showing two views from the configuration flow. All the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
views of the prototype can be seen in [A 5.6]. 
 
Fig. 34 - Two views of the setup flow from the prototype  
96 
==PAGE:105==
 
 
Fig. 35 - Two views of the configuration flow from the prototype. 
 
 
5.4.2. How 
The interactive prototype was evaluated through a usability
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
test in order to investigate if the flow was intuitive for the user
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and if the level of guidance provided was enough. 
  
The tests were carried out as ​Think aloud tests ​(Nielsen, 1993)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
where the participants are asked to communicate their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thoughts and reason their actions while performing them.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Following the think aloud test, a short unstructured interview
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
discussing specific elements of the flow was conducted. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
test was carried out with 5 participants.  
  
To provide context to the test, the participants were asked to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
read a document describing a specific setup scenario, setting
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
the scene before starting the test [A 5.7]. The document
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
described a scenario for why they were setting up the system,
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
while also giving details of how the participants should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
configure the system. In on other words, it provided a specific
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
setup scenario that the participants had to roleplay. In order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
simplify the prototyping process, the interactive prototype was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
designed as a relatively linear path, meaning some buttons or
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
objects were not coded. The scenario was therefore also a way
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
to steer the participants in the right direction and avoid having
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants click on buttons and object that would not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
function. 
97 
==PAGE:106==
 
In order to make the flow more realistic, a paper cut-out of a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
floor plan and the different sensors were provided [A 5.8]. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants could then simulate the placement and installation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the sensors in the various rooms and doors of the house,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
while they were navigating through the prototype on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
computer, see fig. 36.  
 
 
Fig. 36 - Test setup in loop 2: Sensors are getting installed in the house 
 
The tests were documented by screen and audio recording. By
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
doing so, we were able to play back the participants’ cursor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
movement and clicks in the prototype while simultaneously
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hearing the participants verbalise their thoughts.  
 
The action plan for the test were noted down in a test guide [A
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
5.9] in order to have a common script and ensure that all the
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
tests were carried out in the same way. 
5.4.3. Outcome 
In this section the most relevant outcomes of the test will be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented. 
  
The overall flow of the prototype seemed to be perceived as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intuitive by all participants. It made sense for the participants
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
to do actions in the particular order that was designed.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, it was identified that 1 step of the flow seemed to be
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
redundant.
The
intended
flow
of
first
​choosing
sensor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
placements ​and then ​choosing where to start installing was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
perceived as doing the same thing twice. Due to the perceived
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
redundancy, the flow was changed to combine the choice of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor placement and where to start installing, into the same
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
view [A 5.10]. 
  
 
98 
==PAGE:107==
 
A number of usability issues were also identified. The relevant
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
issues identified is listed below: 
 
●
Lack of visual feedback on successful pairing with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor 
●
Clickable lists should not share visual design with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
non-clickable lists. 
●
Text boxes with no clickable options should not share
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
visual design with buttons that are clickable (e.g. in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
define alarms section of the flow) 
●
The configuration flow lacks guidance or descriptions of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
options to better guide the user. 
  
Even though the setup and configuration flow is only supposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
to be done once as part of a ​first time use situation, many of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
actions will be accessible in the UI in order to reconfigure the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system. Therefore, many of the learnings from this loop was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used to refine the dashboard in the next loop. 
 
Based on the outcome from the user tests in this loop an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
updated user requirement list for the proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system was developed, see [A 5.11]. The key learnings from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
loop 2 are summarized in table 5. 
 
 
 
 
Table 5: Key learnings from loop 2 
Key learnings - Loop 2 
-
Flow was perceived as intuitive and key actions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were simple to understand and follow. 
 
-
The set up flow could be improved by adding more
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
guidance and description of options. 
 
-
Text boxes with no clickable options should not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
share visual design with buttons that are clickable. 
 
-
Clickable lists should not share visual design with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
non-clickable lists.
