==PAGE:108==
 
5.5. Loop 3: Refined UI design 
The focus in this loop was to refine the dashboard view based
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on learnings from loop 1 and loop 2, and to choose a visual
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
design aesthetic in order to get one step closer to the final
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interface design. 
5.5.1. What 
In this loop a high-fidelity prototype was made of the UI. This
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
UI is the one enabling the user to conduct the actions and tasks
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in the daily use flow and the reconfiguration use flow. The UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was divided into two parts that should be designed. One of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them being the main dashboard that should enable the user to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
view data and navigate through different views. The other one
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
is the settings menu that enables the user to view current
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
settings and reconfigure the system.  
  
Brand identity  
In order to increase the fidelity of the dashboard a brand
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
identity was developed. First of all, a primary colour was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
selected to be used for the logo and the interface. Choosing the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
right colour for both the brand and the interface is important,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
because it has a great effect on the users’ perception of the
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
product and how they feel when they interact with the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
product. In order to find a fitting colour, the feelings it should
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
evoke were brainstormed: ​Calm, Secure, Respectful, Honesty,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Trust, Love. ​These feelings were used as search terms to find
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
colours and inspiration. The results were collected in a mood
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
board, see fig. 37.  
 
 
Fig. 37 - Color moodboard - the colors are representing the feelings that the 
design are intended to evoke 
The colours of the mood board were added to the dashboard
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design one at a time to experiment with what worked best and
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in the end a dark turquoise also called teal was chosen as the
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
primary colour.  
  
 
 
100 
==PAGE:109==
 
Secondly, a proposal for a name and a logo were created, see
  
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
fig. 38.  
 
 
Fig. 38 - Logo and name proposal 
 
The reasoning behind the name was that it should indicate the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
core functions of the system. Therefore, it was evident to use
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
the word sense, both because the system is based on sensors
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and because it helps the relatives to sense if somethings is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
wrong. Furthermore, it was important that the name should be
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
associated with something positive and friendly in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
represent the product’s caring function. Adding the word
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
friend
gave
the
name
a personal feeling and positive
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
associations. The name Sense Friend, can also associate the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system with a mascot that is helping you. 
  
The reasoning behind the logo was also that it should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
represent the core functions of the system in a simple design. A
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
lot of different logos were sketched out in a brainstorm and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
final logo proposal was chosen to be a sensor icon combined
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
with a heart. 
  
Visual design of main dashboard  
An important part of refining the dashboard was to improve
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the visual design. One of the key design principles behind the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dashboard design was that it should be kept simple and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
minimalistic. A minimalistic design should promote the most
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
screen space to the most relevant information and the visual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aesthetics should not take away focus from the information
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
displayed. When it comes to the content of the dashboard,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
learnings from loop 1 were used as a base for the further
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
development of the dashboard content.  
  
In order to find inspiration, existing dashboard designs were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explored [A 5.12]. Additionally, the visual design layout was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explored by sketching different dashboards designs on paper,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
see fig. 39 and [A 5.13].  
 
 
Fig. 39 - Section of the dashboard ideation sketching 
 
101 
==PAGE:110==
 
Throughout the ideation process the level of information
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
visualised on the main dashboard and the behavioural status
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indication were varied. In order to investigate what the user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would prefer, 2 different dashboard variations were developed
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
[A 5.14]. 
  
Variation 1  
A dashboard with more information, where the focus is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
divided between both the behavioural status indication, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
latest activity and the weekly history view. In this variant the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indication of deviations in behavioural trends is indicated by a
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
smiley-face when there are no deviations and a red question
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
mark if a deviation has been detected, see fig. 40. 
 
Variation 2  
A simple main dashboard where the status of deviation is in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
focus and only the latest activity is shown. In this variant, the
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
status of deviations in behavioural trends is indicated by a red
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
or green ring around a picture of the elderly family member,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
see fig. 41. The idea behind adding a profile picture was to
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
make the dashboard more personalised and to remind the user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that there is person on the other end of the system. 
 
Fig. 40 - Dashboard variant 1 
 
 
 
102 
==PAGE:111==
 
 
 
Fig. 41 - Dashboard variation 2 
 
 
 
Settings menu 
In order to make the dashboard more refined a setting menu
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
was designed. The visual design for the menu was inspired by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
google maps menu and Rejseplanen app menu [A 5.15]. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
frames from the configuration flow in loop 2 were updated
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
based on the learnings from the tests and incorporated into
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
this version. The menu allows the user to access their personal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
settings and change them, see fig. 42.  
 
 
Fig. 42 - Design of the setting menu (left) and the configuration frame for 
custom Alarms (right) 
 
103 
==PAGE:112==
 
 
History and activity log view 
Based on learnings from loop 1, a history view and an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activity log was made accessible from the main dashboard,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
see fig. 43.  
 
Fig. 43 - History view (left) and activity log (right). The views can be 
changed In the bottom bar menu by clicking on the respective icons 
 
The history view provides a bigger picture of how the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
desired insights is changing over a longer period of time,
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
while the activity log can provide context to activity trends
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and be used as a ticket to talk. 
 
All of the designed views were compiled into an interactive
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototype. In order to decrease the time of developing the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototype, only the first dashboard variant was connected to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the menu and all the specific settings were not made fully
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interactive. The second dashboard variant was included in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the prototype in order to enable the users to compare the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
designs. An overview of the prototype and its possible
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interactions can be seen in [A 5.16]. 
5.5.2. How 
With the aim of increasing the fidelity of the prototype it was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
showed on a smartphone. This was done to make it feel like it
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
was a real and functional App and also to test the interface in
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the right size. 
  
The prototype was evaluated through a usability test and a
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
discussion about the user’s preferences. The usability test was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
carried out by handing a phone with the prototype to the
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants and asking them to click around in order to test
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
104 
==PAGE:113==
 
the navigation in the dashboard and in the setting menu. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussion was based on showing the two different dashboard
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
variants
and
asking
which
one
they
prefer
and why.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Furthermore, the participants were asked about their thoughts
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on other parts of the visual design, like the colour and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
visual design. 
An action plan for the test were formulated [A 5.17] in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have a common script and ensure that all the tests were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
carried out in the same way. The prototype and the two
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dashboard
designs
were
tested
and
discussed
with
4
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants. 
5.5.3. Outcome 
In this section the most relevant outcomes of the test will be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented. All of the tests results are described in more detail
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in [A 5.18]. 
  
Initially, all of the participants stated that the prototype looked
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and felt like a real app they could have bought in the app store.
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
This confirmed that the goal of increasing the fidelity of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
prototype had succeeded. 
  
All of the participants liked the minimalistic design and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
colour choice. One participant stated that it was important for
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
him that the design was as simple as possible, so that he could
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
focus on the most important aspect, the well-being of his loved
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
one. This validated the overall visual design and colour choice.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
For that reason, it was decided to bring the visual design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aesthetic and colour choice into the development of the final
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design proposal. 
  
Regarding the structure of the two different dashboard
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
variants, all 4 participants preferred ​variant 2​. Main reasons
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
for the preference towards ​variant 2 was the simplicity of the
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
dashboard layout and the focus on the behavioural deviation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indicator. It was not deemed important for participants to see
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the history view on the main page of the dashboard, when they
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could access it easily from the bottom navigation bar. One of
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the participants reasoned his preference of ​variant 2, with the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
fact that he only needed to know if there were any apparent
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
deviations. In case of a deviation he would then look into the
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
history view or the activity log in order to figure out what was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
going on. In general, he was not interested in being presented
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with data that he would have to interpret himself, but rather to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
use the histographic data and the activity log to put a deviation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
warning into context. Based on this outcome it was decided to
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
use the structure of dashboard 2 in the final design.  
  
The participants’ preferences were more diverging when it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
came to the profile picture of the elderly displayed on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dashboard. 2 participants were indifferent about having a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
picture displayed. One of the participants mentioned that she
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would expect her mother to become annoyed if she wanted to
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
105 
==PAGE:114==
 
take a picture of her for the dashboard. On the other hand, one
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participant really liked the idea of a profile picture, as he
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
thought it made the dashboard more personal. The participant
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
compared the profile picture to having a picture of your
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
children displayed on your phone when receiving a call from
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
them, and that it was something that made him happy. Based
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on this learning it was decided to make the dashboard function
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as default without a picture, but that it should accommodate
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
the possibility of adding a picture, if a user so desired. 
  
Regarding the usability and navigation of the interface it was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
found that some of the features were too difficult to see. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
text in the bottom bar menu, the time and type of activity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
displayed under the latest activity and the menu icons were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mentioned as parts that were difficult to see. In order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
improve the final design these features should increase in size
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and/or contrast.  
  
Besides the above mentioned outcomes, the participants also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
had specific ideas for improvement. For instance, 2 of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants mentioned that they would, in case of a deviation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
being detected, prefer the deviation pop up to be shown as a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
default when you open the app. This way the participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would not be in doubt as they would not be presented with the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
normal view of the dashboard. 
  
Another interesting idea mentioned was to add an extra level
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of deviation status. For instance, the frame around the profile
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
picture could display a yellow colour if a minor deviation had
 
 
  
 
   
 
 
 
been detected and then turn red if the deviation exceeds a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
critical level or green again if the activity goes back to normal.  
  
Furthermore, one of the participants had an idea about how to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
improve the overview of the sensors. He suggested that the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
battery status should be green, yellow or red and there should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be a clear indication if one of the sensors did not work as
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
supposed to.  
  
Based on the outcome from the user tests in this loop an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
updated user requirement list for the proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system was developed, see [A 5.19]. The key learnings from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
loop 3 are summarized in table 6. 
 
 
 
106 
==PAGE:115==
 
Table 6: Key learnings from loop 3 
Key learnings - Loop 3 
-
The participants liked the overall visual design 
and color choice. 
 
-
All of the participants preferred the simple 
structure of dashboard 2. 
 
-
A profile picture is a personal preference and both 
scenarios should be possible. 
 
-
Some features of the interface were to small and 
difficult to read or notice. 
 
-
Resolution of deviation detection on the main 
dashboard could be increased by adding an 
indication of minor deviation.

5.6. The final UI design 
Based on the learnings gained through the three loops one
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
final UI design was developed. The final UI proposal is both
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
showcasing the identified user needs and requirements for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed
monitoring
system
and
can
be
used
as
a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
demonstration of the proposed monitoring system in a future
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
perspective. In this section, the design choices and views of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
final UI will be presented. For an overview of the essential UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
views see [A 5.20]. 
  
Dashboard: Home view 
It was decided to continue with the second dashboard variant
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
developed in loop 3, as this was the one all of the participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
preferred. The participants especially liked the simplicity of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the design that put emphasis on the behavioural trend
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indicator. In order to increase the focus even more the colour
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indicating frame around the picture/smiley were made slightly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
thicker, see fig. 44. 
  
Another key learning was that adding a profile picture of the
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
elderly should be an option, but the dashboard should also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
function without it. Therefore, the final proposal has a version
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
with a profile picture and one without. In the version without a
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
profile picture a smiley is used as a placeholder in the normal
 
  
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
activity scenario, as it brings positive associations, see fig. 44
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
(right). 
 
Fig. 44: Final UI proposal: The two possible dashboard editions, with 
picture (left) and without picture (right). 
 
 
108 
==PAGE:117==
 
Three stages of behavioural feedback 
The idea of adding a yellow indication of minor deviation,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
proposed in loop 3, was considered and discussed before
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
implementing it in the final design; 
When only having 2 stages of feedback - Green and red, the
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
dashboard could for instance indicate normal activity ​(green),
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
while in reality the behavioural trends of the elderly person
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were negatively deviating. The dashboard would in that case
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
keep on indicating that activity is normal until a certain
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviation-threshold is reached, thus going from indicating
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
normal activity ​to ​critical deviation in an instant. The binary
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
approach of indicating behavioural deviations might therefore
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be lacklustre in format, and lead to misunderstandings and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
unpleasant surprises.  
  
The hypothesis is therefore; ​by ​increasing the resolution of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
feedback it is possible to provide a more nuanced feedback to the
   
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
user and avoid misunderstandings, hence increasing the value of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
the interface​. 
  
Therefore, it was decided to implement a middle stage,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indicated by a yellow frame and the text ​Minor deviation, ​see
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fig. 45 (middle)​. ​This change will enable the user to have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
actionable information at an earlier stage of deviation.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However it will possibly change the way the ​critical deviation
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(red) indication is perceived by the user. By adding the ​minor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviation indication, the ​critical deviation indication will most
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
likely be perceived as more severe. The change in perception
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
coming as an effect of the ​critical deviation indication being an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
additional
step
further
away
from
the
​normal activity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indication. The threshold for a ​critical deviation ​should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
therefore be increased accordingly. 
In order to increase the importance of the feedback of a critical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
deviation a red exclamation mark was put on top of the profile
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
picture and a pop up with details and instant call function is
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
shown when the app is opened, see fig. 45 (right).  
 
Fig. 45: Final UI proposal: Green, yellow and red color indicating the 
status of deviation in behavioral trends (with picture). 
 
109 
==PAGE:118==
 
For the dashboard version without a profile picture, a question
 
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
mark was used to indicate the middle stage of deviation, see
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fig. 46. The idea behind using a question mark was to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communicate the fact that it is not really good or really bad,
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
but somewhere in between.  
 
Fig. 46: Final UI proposal: green, yellow and red color indicating the 
status of deviation in behavioral trends (without picture). 
 
History view 
In loop 1 it was learned that it can be valuable for the users to
 
   
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
see both a weekly overview and a bigger picture (e.g. monthly
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
to yearly). Therefore, it was chosen to incorporate both views
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
in the history view, accessible from the main dashboard view.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Furthermore, it was made possible to switch between the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
desired insights in a top bar menu, see fig. 47. 
 
Fig. 47: Final UI proposal: The history view with both weekly and monthly 
overview. Activity level (left) and visits (right) 
 
 
110 
==PAGE:119==
 
Sensor status feedback  
The feedback of the sensor status was improved by adding a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
better indication of the battery level and connection of each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor. If a sensor is low on battery or is missing connection,
   
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
feedback will be given in the form of a red warning symbol
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
both in the menu and in the sensor overview, showing which
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
room the sensor is placed in, see fig. 48 (left). 
 
 
Fig. 48: Final UI proposal: Warning symbol on sensor overview (left) and 
sensor battery and signal status (right) 
By clicking at the room in the sensor overview, the user can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
access a list of all the sensors installed in the room and see
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which of the sensors that should be checked, see fig. 48 (right).  
 
Activity log 
The readability in the activity log was improved by increasing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the contrast and making the text black, see fig. 49.  
 
 
Fig. 49: Final UI proposal: Increased contrast of the text and background in 
the activity log for better readability 
111 
==PAGE:120==
 
 
 
Alarms 
Lastly, the final alarm views were designed. The relative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
should receive notifications in cases where a fall is detected, a
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
  
personal alarm is triggered or a critical deviation in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
behavioural trends is detected.  
  
If a fall is detected, the relative should have the possibility to
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
call the elderly directly from the notification view, in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
figure out the severity of the fall, see fig. 50 (left). When
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
swiping right on the notification, the app is entered, see fig. 50
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
(right). The red colour and exclamation mark should symbolise
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that the case can be critical.  
  
When a personal alarm is triggered, the notification message
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
should be less serious, see fig. 51 (left). Though, it should still
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
be possible to enter the app directly in order to make a call to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
the elderly to check up on them. In the case where a personal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
alarm is triggered, the frame around the picture will remain
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
green if no deviation in the behavioural trend has been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
detected, see fig. 51 (right). 
 
 
Figure 50: Final UI proposal: Notification is pushed if a fall is detected (left) 
and the app’s home view is changed (right). 
 
 
112 
==PAGE:121==
 
 
 
Figure 51: Final dashboard proposal: Notification is pushed if a personal 
alarm is triggered (left) and the app’s home view is changed (right). 
 
 
113 
==PAGE:122==
 
5.7. Discussion 
One of the main focus points of the final UI design is the status
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
indication of behavioural trends. The concept revolves around
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
an advanced algorithm being able to detect deviations in an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly person's behaviour. In the thesis team the concept of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
detecting deviations in behavioural trends were discussed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
many times. What behaviour patterns are relevant to analyse?
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
what determines whether something is considered a minor
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
deviation or a critical deviation? What if a person’s “normal”
 
  
 
 
   
 
 
behaviour pattern changes over time? To answer these
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questions we could only guess. Designing such an algorithm is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
not expected to be an easy task, but for the purpose of this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project such an algorithm was deemed to be feasible. However,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
if the final UI design presented in this chapter was to be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
developed further and made into a reality, one of the biggest
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
challenges is expected to be the development of this algorithm.
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
It was concluded that in the event of going forward with the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project, it would be wise to involve healthcare professionals
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
such as geriatric doctors and mathematical experts in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
research and development of such an algorithm. 
  
After loop 3, only minor changes were requested from the test
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants. Thus it was safe to assume that the final design
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposal presented in this chapter encapsulates the needs and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirements of the users involved in the tests. However, it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
not possible to say that the final design proposal fulfils all
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives needs and requirements. Before starting an actual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
development of the proposal, it would be smart to validate the
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
UI design in a test run of larger scale. For instance, it would be
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
interesting to test if relatives living in other parts of Denmark,
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
or even other countries, would have similar requirements.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Perhaps a relative in Finland worries about other things than a
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
relative in Spain does. Ultimately, the design work done in this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project, is a product of the users that were involved. In the end
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it is hard to evaluate if it was the right users to involve. The
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
obvious way to evaluate would therefore be to involve even
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more users.  
5.8. Reflection 
In loop 1, 3 different dashboard variations were prototyped. It
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
  
was a conscious design choice to keep the fidelity of the 3
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
variations low, amongst other reasons; in order to build them
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
quickly. However, designing and building prototypes of the 3
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
variations took longer time than initially expected. A reason for
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
this might be that it was decided to build the prototypes in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Adobe XD, enabling the prototypes to be interactive. However,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
when
looking
back
at
test done in loop 1, including
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interactivity in the prototypes did not make a big difference.
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
Also the visual design fidelity of the prototypes could most
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
114 
==PAGE:123==
 
likely have been even lower, without changing the outcome of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the tests significantly. For instance, the prototypes for loop 1
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
could have been made with sketches on paper or cut-outs of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cardboard, in order to reduce the time spend in this loop.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, for loop 2 and 3, it made sense to have a higher
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
amount of interactivity, as the usability was one of the focus
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
points of the tests.  
5.9. Conclusions 
In this chapter, the user needs and requirements of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed monitoring system, dubbed “SenseFriend”, was
 
 
 
 
 
 
further uncovered. This was achieved through 3 loops of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
designing, prototyping and user testing of UI designs. Finally,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the learnings were consolidated and synthesized into a final
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
design proposal for the UI of the proposed monitoring system.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The proposed UI design has a minimalistic and simple layout
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
with a teal colour theme. The dashboard view of the UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
revolves
around
a
behavioural
trend
status
indicator,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communicated by a green, yellow or red coloured ring.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Furthermore, recent activities recorded by the system is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
displayed in a log format and a historical data is presented
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
with graphs. 
 
 
 
 
115 
==PAGE:124==
 
6. DISCUSSION 
In this chapter, the discussion phase is presented. The aim with
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
this phase was to discuss likely benefits and challenges related
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to the proposed system on both a micro, meso and macro level,
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
with the aim of understanding what impacts the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system could have on the society. 
 
 
 
 
 
116 
==PAGE:125==
 
6.1. Methodology 
The approach taken to discuss likely societal impacts of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed monitoring system, has been to view the impacts on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3 different societal levels. The approach is inspired by the 3
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
levels of analysis often used in social sciences; Micro, Meso and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Macro​.  
The micro, meso and macro levels can be used in many
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
different contexts of the society. In this discussion they have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
been used in the following way:  
On the ​Micro level, impacts of the proposed system are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussed in relation to the primary users, the relative and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly. On the ​Meso ​level, impacts related to the municipalities
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and their home care processes are discussed. On the ​Macro
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
level, socio-economic impacts are discussed in relation to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
modern welfare society of Denmark. Relevant benefits and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
challenges of all 3 levels are presented and discussed in the
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
following.  
6.2. Micro 
Benefits 
Peace of mind for relatives 
The core aim of developing the system is to bring peace of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mind for the relative. Many relatives naturally involve
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
themselves in the care of their parents or in-laws. Even though
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it is natural for the relatives to help their elderly family
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
member, the user research of this thesis have shown that it can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
be challenging and end up causing a lot of worry. The proposed
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system can support the relatives with data about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the elderly’s behavioural trends and daily activities and warn
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them about deviations and critical events. In this way the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system can alleviate worries and speculations, hence reduce
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some of the challenges relatives face when becoming involved
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in the care of the elderly. This can give the relatives a surplus
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
of mental resources, that can be shared among children, grand
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
children and their job. Ultimately leading to an improved
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
quality of life.  
  
Peace of mind for elderly 
The main benefit for the elderly will also be an improved peace
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of mind. A key takeaway from the background investigation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and the user research, was that many elderlies worry about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
falling
and
that
many
elderlies,
who
have
previously
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experienced illness, worry about deterioration. The proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system will likely give these elderly a sense of
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
security, knowing that a relative will be notified if they fall or if
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
their behavioural patterns changes dramatically. In this regard,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
an interesting aspect in the future development of the system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would be to incorporate a UI for the elderly. In this way, the
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly could track their daily activities themself, hence
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
providing a sense of empowerment. This will become more
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
117 
==PAGE:126==
 
valuable in the future, where the older generations will be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experts in using smartphones.  
  
Extend time of independent living 
Another, more clinical, benefit for the elderly is the fact that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system can help extending the time of living independently
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in their own home. Detecting critical deviations in behavioural
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
trends, will most likely make it possible to intervene early and
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
avoid serious deterioration. As a result, it will be possible to
 
 
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
avoid or postpone long term illness and hospitalisation and in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
worst case death. In the same regard, the increased sense of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
safety, the system can provide, might cause a decline in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
elderlies’ fear of falling. In the background research, it was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
found that the fear of falling can increase the risk of falling.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Hence the system might reduce the risk of falling, and thereby
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reduce the risk for complications related to falling. Ultimately,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
leading to postponing the need for extensive care (e.g. moving
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
nursing home) and thereby extending the time of the elderly’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
independence. 
  
Improved relationship 
An extra benefit of the proposed system could be an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
improvement of the relationship between the relative and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly. Today, it is in many cases hard for relatives to figure
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
out how the elderly is doing. They have to question the elderly
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and it might feel like an interrogation. One of the interviewed
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives explained concerns about always asking the same
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questions when calling (e.g. how are you feeling, did you get
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dinner), speculating that it might be annoying for the elderly to
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
always get asked how they were doing. However, with the use
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the proposed system, the relatives could be informed about
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
this
through
the
UI.
Thereby
changing the subject of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conversations from the health of the elderly towards other
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topics. 
 
Cost savings 
The economic benefits of the proposed system on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
individual level will not be a case in countries like Denmark,
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
due to universal healthcare insurance. However, in a country
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
like USA, where people have to pay for healthcare themselves,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the proposed monitoring system could have economic benefits
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for both the elderly and the relative. The use of a monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
system might lead to cost savings by avoiding or postponing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospitalization and the need for extensive home care.  
 
Challenges 
False-negative risk  
A challenge with the proposed system is that it can lead to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
false-negative cases. A false-negative is defined as a result that
 
  
  
 
  
 
 
appears negative when it should not. In this context being that
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system states that everything is normal in a situation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
where it should have detected something critical. Therefore, in
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
118 
==PAGE:127==
 
order to avoid false-negative cases, it is very important to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
develop the system as robust as possible. However, technology
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
can never be 100 % reliable, as there will always be a chance of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
human errors and inadequate use. For that reason, it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
important that the functions of the system are communicated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
clearly to both the relative and the elderly. This is assumed to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
be a challenging aspect, as it requires the system providers to
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
communicate the technology in a way the relative can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understand. It is really important that the relative understand
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
the technology, as they are the one to pass the information on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to the elderly in a way they understand. The challenging aspect
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
is that there are many links where information can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
misunderstood, hence increasing the risk of inadequate use of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system.  
 
If the relatives or the elderly have blind faith in the system, it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
can lead to “dangerous” situations. For instance, a situation
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
might arise, in which the system will not detect a critical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
deviation, giving the relative an idea of everything being
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alright. Where in reality the elderly is doing bad, but does not
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
bother asking for help, as they think it will be detected by the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
system.  
 
Less social interaction  
Another possible challenge with the system is that it could
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cause a decline in the social interaction between the relative
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and the elderly. This was a concern shared among many of the
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives interviewed in the user research. The relatives
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questioned whether they would call or visit their parents less if
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
they had a monitoring system. Visits and calls from relatives
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
might be the only social interaction for many elderly people
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
living alone. In an analysis of loneliness among elderly, made
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
by KL, it was found that a higher number of visits from their
 
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
children had a positive effect on the elderlies’ degree of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
loneliness (KL, 2018). This shows that this is a risk that should
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
be mitigated. However, some of the interviewed relative also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expressed that the system could improve the social interaction
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with the elderly, as the system provides information to talk
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
about. Furthermore, the information about the current activity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the elderly can give the relative and idea of when it is best to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
visit the elderly. Hence improving the circumstances for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increased social interaction.  
6.3. Meso 
Benefits 
Replacing minor home care needs  
By offering the proposed system as a private commercial
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solution, it can still have some beneficial effects for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
municipalities. One being that the system could replace or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reduce home care services related to oversight and so called
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
safety visits (tryghedsbesøg). With the implementation of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system, relatives would not have to worry about the elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
lying helpless on the floor. Thus, implementing the system can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
119 
==PAGE:128==
 
postpone the demand for home care until a certain point. For
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
instance, until a point where the elderly will need help with
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
daily activities of living. For the municipality, this would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
effectively reduce the total costs of home care per citizen.  
  
Relatives might take over care services  
A likely effect of the proposed monitoring system will be that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives will engage more in the care of the elderly. For
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
instance, if an elderly shows signs of minor behavioural
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviations, a relative will likely take action, and figure out if
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
something can be done. This could involve the relative taking
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the elderly out for more walks and beginning to help the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly with activities such as grocery shopping and so on. A
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
hypothesis is, that it is more likely for a relative with access to
 
 
   
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
knowledge of the elderly’s state of health to intervene and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
provide care, than for a relative without access to such
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
knowledge. If relatives start taking it upon themselves to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
provide
support
and
perhaps
some form of care, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
municipality will benefit from not having to spend resources
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on such care services. An indirect effect of this, is also a likely
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
 
increase in social interaction between the elderly and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relative, alleviating feelings of loneliness. 
 
Postpone the need for extensive care 
By implementing a monitoring system, it is likely that some
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
types of home care services or that staying at a nursing home,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
could be postponed. The main hypothesis behind this claim is,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that if the proposed system succeeds in detecting signs of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deterioration
early
enough,
long
term
illness
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospitalisation
can
be
avoided.
Long
term
illness
and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospitalisation can lead to serious decline in the elderly’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
functional abilities, hence increasing the need for home care.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Furthermore, the system’s detection of falls and other critical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
events, will make the elderly feel safer, empowering the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly to keep living in their own home for longer. Ultimately,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
this can postpone the need of moving to a nursing home.  
 
Granted by the municipality as a home care service 
In a case where a monitoring system can provide the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
economical benefits mentioned above, the municipality will
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have a natural interest in implementing such systems in the
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
homes of its elderly citizens. Therefore, it could also be a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
possibility that the proposed system could be granted through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the municipality as a replacement of home care services. The
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
first steps in this direction has already been taken by the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
municipality of Aalborg. 
In an interview with Lars Nørh, chief consultant of quality and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
innovation in the elder care sector of Aalborg municipality, he
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explains that they see a potential benefit in granting a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system instead of a home care service (see [A 2.3]
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
for interview notes). Another interesting insight from Lars
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Nørh was that even though they are testing a monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
120 
==PAGE:129==
 
system targeted the home care professionals, some of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relatives have shown interest in receiving the information in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
order to use it as a private solution. Because of this, Lars Nørh
 
 
  
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was not dismissive of a solution where the municipality would
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
grant a monitoring system targeted the relatives. However,
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
Lars Nøhr also explained that they will have to investigate the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cost-effectiveness of this scenario, before knowing if it would
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
be worthwhile to grant a monitoring system instead of a home
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
  
 
care service.  
  
Furthermore,
by
getting
involved
in
the
provision
of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring systems, the municipality will have the ability to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
influence which systems that will be implemented in their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
citizens’ homes. Thus, making sure that the systems installed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are reliable and of a certain standard, instead of relying on the
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
citizens to choose the right system. 
 
Big data 
In a case where hundreds of houses in a community are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
equipped with the proposed monitoring system, it would be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
possible to create a sensor network capable of “diagnosing” an
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
entire community. By having a network of sensors spread out
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
in a community, vast amounts of activity and frailty related
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
data could be collected and analysed. From analysing the data,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
community wide patterns and trends could be identified and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used by the municipality to, for instance, prioritize certain
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
things in elder-care budgeting and planning. However, this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would only be possible if the monitoring systems are granted
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
by the municipality and if it the user accept that the monitored
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
data may be shared with the municipality. 
 
Challenges 
Data insights being ignored by municipalities 
If the proposed monitoring system is sold as a private
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
commercial solution to the users, it can potentially cause
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conflicts between the users and the municipality. If the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system indicate that the elderly is deteriorating,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the users might be inclined to request more home care or that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the elderly should be transferred to a nursing home. But if the
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
  
 
municipality does not recognise the proposed system as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
showing truthful data, users can feel that the municipality is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
wrongfully ignoring the signs of deterioration. Therefore, it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
important for the providers of the monitoring system to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
establish some sort of partnership with municipalities in order
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to accommodate such conflicts.  
 
Require technical support  
In a case where a municipality grants the proposed solution to
  
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
citizens as an alternative home care service, the municipality
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
will also have an obligation to help the citizens with technical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
support. The extent of the technical support needed for such a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
monitoring system will be hard to estimate. In such a case it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
   
highly recommended that the municipality will set high
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
121 
==PAGE:130==
 
demands to the system providers regarding system uptime.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
For the municipality, picking the right partners to deliver such
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a system is of utmost importance. 
 
Obligation to intervene 
In a case where the municipality gets access to data through a
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
monitoring system like the one proposed, a moral challenge
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
might arise. If a system is able to provide robust evidence that
 
   
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
a citizen is deteriorating, the municipality will feel obligated to
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intervene. Having access to such knowledge might mean that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more
cases
of
deterioration
will
be
discovered.
The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
municipality will in that case have to allocate more resources
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to help the deteriorating citizens. In itself it is great that the
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
citizens in need will receive the help they need. But the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
municipality might end up having an increase in citizens that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
need care, thus spending more resources, when the opposite
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was the intention. 
6.4. Macro 
Benefits 
In the ageing society we face today, a rising demand on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
healthcare and elder care will cause immense economic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
pressure on the welfare society. In a 2018 report from VIVE,
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
the future healthcare spending in Denmark is expected to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increase from around 160 billion DKK in 2018 to around 200
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
billion DKK in 2050, due to the demographic effects (Iversen &
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
Kjellberg, 2018). A solution like the proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system will likely be able to alleviate some of the economic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
pressure caused by the ageing society. A number of likely
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
effects of the proposed monitoring system, that can reduce
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
public spending will be presented here. 
  
Less spending on elder care 
The spending on elder care will likely be reduced by: 
●
Postponing the need for extensive care, in the form of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
home care and nursing homes (see section 6.3). 
●
Involving relatives in more care activities, e.g. providing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
oversight (see section 6.3). 
 
Less spending on healthcare 
Early detection of critical events such as falls, will likely reduce
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the degree of deterioration the fall incident can cause, hence
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reducing the need for healthcare. Without a monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system, an elderly person that had fallen, will in some cases
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
end up lying helpless on the floor for many hours. A wide range
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
of complications could then follow, not from the fall itself, but
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from lying on the floor for long periods, e.g. lying on a cold
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
floor for many hours might lead to pneumonia. Therefore,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
being able to intervene early, before extra complications of a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
fall develop, will reduce the need for healthcare of the elderly. 
122 
==PAGE:131==
 
In the same regard, detecting deviations in behavioural trends
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
might
also
help
reduce
healthcare
spending.
Detecting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviations might enable early interventions that in turn can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
help the elderly avoid long term illness and hospitalisation. In
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospitals today, systems like Early Warning Scores (EWS), are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used to detect deviations in vital parameters. By detecting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deviations, the hospital staff is able to do early clinical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interventions in order to avoid serious patient deterioration.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The hypothesis is, that by intervening early, lives can be saved
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and future spending can be greatly reduced. Something which
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would also apply to an in home monitoring setting. 
 
Less spending on fall incidents 
In the background investigation, it was found that the fear of
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
falling may lead to higher risk of falling. If the elderly’s anxiety
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
about falling can be reduced, with the implementation of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed monitoring system (as also described in section 6.2),
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the number of fall incidents might also be reduced. In the end,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reducing resources spent on treating fall incidents and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
possible complications of falls. 
 
Challenges 
Imbalance in society 
If the proposed monitoring system is sold as a private
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
commercial solution, it can cause imbalance in society.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Depending on the price of the system, some citizens might not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be able to afford it. In this case, an imbalance can occur if
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
wealthy citizens, due to owning a monitoring system, will be
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
able to demonstrate their need for more home care. This is yet
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
another reason why it would be beneficial to grant the system
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
through the municipality, enabling all citizens a possibility to
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
acquire
the
system.
However,
starting
at
the
private
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
commercial market might be a good idea in order to develop
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
and mature the system, thereby reducing the price over time.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
An example of this is the robotic vacuum cleaner. The robotic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
vacuum cleaner, was initially a luxury product, too expensive
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
for many households. But, as the market demand started to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increase, more companies began to produce and sell the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
product and the price decreased immensely. The decrease in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
price made the product affordable for the municipality, that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
gladly adopted it as a tool to relieve the home care cleaning
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
services.
The
same
scenario could likely occur in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
perspective of in home monitoring systems. 
 
Political discussion  
Even though the system is designed with the intention to help
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
and relieve the relatives, some may perceive it as an imposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
responsibility for the relatives. The relatives’ responsibility to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
help with the care of an elderly family member has been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussed for many years in Denmark. According to Ældre
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Sagen, there is two contrary opinions on the matter. Some
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
believe that the elderly should have the right to receive care
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
without the involvement of their relatives, because they have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
123 
==PAGE:132==
 
paid taxes throughout their entire lives. Another opinion is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that it is innate for relatives to help and take responsibility
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
when their family members grow old, and that it should be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
natural for the relatives to step in, when the amount of elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increase in the society (Ældre Sagen, 2016). Consequently, it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
expected that the implementation of a monitoring systems
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
targeted the relatives would be a subject for discussion,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
whether it is as a private commercial solution or as a solution
   
  
 
 
 
 
  
 
granted by the municipality.  
 
6.5. Reflection 
In this chapter, the most relevant benefits and challenges, from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the thesis teams point of view, have been reflected upon.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, before a system like this should be developed and
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
introduced to the market, a more thorough impact and risk
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
analysis should be carried out. By doing so, it will be possible
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
to verify whether or not the system can provide the intended
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
value and. Furthermore, it will be uncovered what challenges
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
can be mitigated through the design of the system. In order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
investigate the impacts more thoroughly, we recommend that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
external stakeholders should be involved. These could for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
instance be some of the identified contextual stakeholders in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
section 3.4 of chapter 3. Especially, Ældre Sagen could be an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interesting stakeholder to involve, as relatives of elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
people is one of their key interests. During the last phase of the
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project, we attempted to get in contact with the persons
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
responsible for the field of relatives in Ældre Sagen. Our intent
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was to hear an expert opinion about the likely impacts of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposed monitoring system. Specifically, what likely impacts
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the system could have on relatives and the elderly as well as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their internal relation. Unfortunately, it was not possible to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
establish contact. It was chosen to postpone establishing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
contact to Ældre Sagen until a later phase of the project, in
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
order to have a specific system design to present. In retrospect,
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it could have been beneficial to establish contact earlier in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project, as it would have increased the timeframe for response.  
 
 
124 
==PAGE:133==
 
6.6. Conclusion 
In this chapter, the likely impacts of the proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system have been discussed. Identified benefits and challenges
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on a micro-, meso-, and macro level of the society are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented and discussed. On the micro level, the benefits of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
creating peace of mind and empowering elderly to stay
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
independent for longer are discussed. Furthermore, challenges
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relating to social contact and robustness of the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system is discussed.  
On the meso level it is discussed how municipalities can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
benefit from reducing and postponing the need for home care,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as well as how municipalities can benefit from offering the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system as a granted home care service. Challenges
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
related
to
the
involvement of the municipality in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
procurement and data collection of the monitoring system is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
also discussed. 
On the macro level, it is discussed how the proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system might be beneficial for a welfare society by
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
reducing spending on elder care and healthcare. Finally,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
challenges related to how the proposed system can enter the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
market, and the political perception of the system are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussed. 
 
 
 
 
125 
==PAGE:134==
 
7. OUTRO 
In this chapter, the thesis project will be closed through a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
reflection of the overall process and a conclusion on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project as a whole. 
7.1. Process reflection 
The individual phases of the project have been discussed and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reflected upon throughout the report, in their respective
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
chapters. Therefore, the aim of this section is to reflect upon
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the process in general. Looking back at the thesis project, there
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are many decisions and activities that can be reflected upon.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, three major points for reflection were found
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
especially interesting to go in detail with; 
7.1.1. Change of project scope  
The scope of the project has changed throughout the process
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from focusing on both the UI design and the industrial design,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to limiting the focus on just the UI design. Even though the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project proposal revolved around designing a UI for a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
monitoring system, we were motivated to explore and develop
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a more holistic solution. Therefore, the initial project plan [A
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
1.1] was divided in 6 phases, including a product design phase,
 
 
 
  
 
  
 
 
 
with the aim of exploring the industrial design of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensor-system. However, as the project evolved and the UI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design phase was about to start we began to reconsider the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
initial plan. We realised that the time allocated for the interface
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design was too scarce to carry out the intended 3 loops of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
design, prototyping and user testing. This forced us to either
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
revise the plan or to compromise on the depth of the UI design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phase. Ultimately, we choose to eliminate the product design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phase from the thesis, which resulted in the final project plan
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with 5 phases instead of 6 [A 1.2]. This change allowed us to
  
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have time enough to develop a more detailed and well tested
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
design proposal for a UI, which was the core of the project
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proposal given in the first place. However, we still recommend
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that the industrial design of the sensor devices could be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
valuable to explored in a future project. 
7.1.2. Project management 
Throughout the project, a Gantt chart has been the main
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
management tool for keeping track of the project. The Gantt
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
chart has been valuable for the initial planning of the project
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and to make sure that the overall time plan was kept.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Furthermore, the Gantt chart has provided a good overview of
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
the process and high level tasks of the different phases.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Without the Gantt chart it would have been difficult to assess if
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
  
the project was ahead or behind schedule. However, the Gantt
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
chart has not been sufficient for managing the individual tasks
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
within each phase. Looking back, a tool like Trello could have
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
126 
==PAGE:135==
 
been useful for planning and managing each phase while
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
working on it. While working on a big project like the master
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
thesis, the workload did sometimes seem immense and it was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
difficult to keep track of loose ends. Especially in the last two
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
weeks of the project, where the focus was the thesis writing,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
we have spent substantial time on making to do lists for each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
day. This was done in order to make sure that important parts
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the thesis were not neglected in a time under pressure. This
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
has shown how important it is to spend time on establishing a
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
  
management strategy from an early point in the project.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Therefore, a learning for future projects is to remember to
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
allocate time for project planning and management earlier in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the project plan. 
7.1.3. User involvement 
Throughout the project, user involvement has been a key
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
focus. From the beginning of the project we have been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proactive in finding users, by developing a recruitment
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
strategy as one of the first tasks of the project. It turned out to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
be a harder and more time consuming task to recruit users and
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
setup interviews, than initially anticipated. The initial strategy
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
involved sharing recruitment flyers in our network, both
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
through digital and analogue channels. However, it did not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
grant substantial results. Likely reasons for this, is that we did
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
not target the right forums for recruitment, that the target user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
group were highly specific (worried relatives of a frail elderly
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
person living alone), and the fact that we could only
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
compensate the participants with our gratitude. In the end it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
was possible to setup 7 interviews with relatives. The initial
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
strategy for reaching out to and setting up interviews with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly people living alone, was to go through the relatives. It
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
turned out to be difficult to make an actual appointment
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
through the relatives, and shaped up to become a time
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
consuming process, involving a lot of scheduling and travel
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
time. Luckily, we received an offer to come and spend the next
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
morning in one of the many eldercare activity centres we had
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
emailed. It turned out to be a perfect place to find the target
  
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
users as the majority of the elderly in the activity centres
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experienced some form of frailty and were living alone. During
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the UI design phase, user tests were conducted in 3 loops. It
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
  
would have been preferable to have included new participants
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for each loop of testing. By including new participants in each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
loop, the UI design and the sensor-system behind could have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
been validated with more potential users. However, because of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the difficulty in recruiting participants, many of the same
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants took part in all 3 loops of testing. Thus, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
insights gained through the 3 loops were based on more or less
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the same participants. However, due to the time constraint of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the project, the recruitment of more participants would have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
taken valuable time from the design process. Resulting in less
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
loops of development and testing. Hence, it was decided to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
utilise the contacts already established, in order to have time
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for more loops and refinement of the UI design.  
127 
==PAGE:136==
 
7.2. Conclusion 
In this project, a user centric design thinking approach has
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
been used to explore a UI design, exploiting ambient in-home
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
sensors, aimed at providing peace of mind for relatives
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
worrying about a frail elderly family member. Throughout the
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
project, user needs and requirements have been uncovered by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conducting semi-structured interviews with 7 relatives and 14
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
elderlies and through 3 loops of prototype facilitated user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
tests. The learnings from the interviews and the user tests,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were subsequently used to develop a UI design proposal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fulfilling the essential user needs and requirements identified
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in this project. 
  
The final UI proposal dubbed SenseFriend is a minimalistic teal
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
coloured App for smartphones. The SenseFriend interface
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
revolves around a dashboard view containing a status
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
indicator, communicating the level of deviation in behavioural
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
trends, and the latest detected activity. The status indicator is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
comprised of a green, yellow or red coloured ring with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
complementary text indicating 3 different states of behavioural
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
trends; Normal activity, Minor deviation and Critical deviation.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
From the dashboard an activity log and a history view can be
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
accessed, which enables the user to view the activities of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
latest 24 hours as well as weekly and monthly trends of  
 
specified activities. Besides the main views, the UI contains a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
menu, enabling the user to configure insights, custom alerts,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sensors and profile settings. Furthermore, SenseFriend can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
push alarms to users, in cases where a fall is detected, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
customized alarm is triggered or a critical deviation in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
behavioural trend is detected.  
  
Finally, the likely societal impacts of the proposed monitoring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
system have been discussed on a micro-, meso- and macro
 
 
 
 
  
 
 
 
 
level. The highlighted likely impacts on the micro level being
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
an improved peace of mind for both the relative and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elderly, empowering the elderly to stay independent for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
longer. On a meso and macro level this can result in savings for
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
both the municipalities and society in general, as the elderly’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
need for both homecare and healthcare will likely decrease or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be postponed.
